Key Theological Issues

(1) Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 17‚Äì20; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. a)

(1) ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü Joseph C. HoareÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö17-20Ôºõ St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d aÔºâ

Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü, 1851‚Äì1906)

Joseph C. Hoare was the fourth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1898‚Äì1906). Born in 1851, he graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained into the clergy. Sent by the Church Missionary Society (CMS) to China, Hoare established Trinity Theological School in Ningbo, Zhejiang, where he served as principal. During his 20 years in Ningbo, he became proficient in the Ningbo dialect and developed a unique educational model, ‚Äúevangelical theory through ambulando‚Äù (‚Äúlearning theology through missionary practice‚Äù). This method emphasized hands-on training, with Hoare personally leading students on preaching journeys to remote villages. These missions often lasted up to ten weeks, blending demonstration and instruction. Under his leadership, Trinity Theological School trained dozens of Chinese missionaries, becoming a premier institution in the region.

On June 11, 1898, Hoare was consecrated as the Bishop of Victoria at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral in London and was posted to Hong Kong.

In 1901, Hoare reorganized St. Paul‚Äôs College into a theological seminary focused on training Chinese clergy. He reached an agreement with the CMS, whereby the bishop directly managed the teaching while the CMS provided additional staff and resources. During his tenure, the Chinese Anglican community in Hong Kong expanded, adding Holy Trinity Church and All Saints‚Äô Church alongside St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. Hoare also established the Chinese Anglican Church Body in 1902, which was incorporated as a legal entity under his leadership as chairman.

In September 1906, Hoare led four theology students from St. Paul‚Äôs College (Ho Dao-pei, Cheng Yung-chi, Leung Kam-po, and Wong Ting-sang) on a missionary trip to Castle Peak by sea. On their return journey on September 18, they encountered the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon,‚Äù which capsized their boat. Both Hoare and the students perished, and their bodies were never recovered. Only Hoare‚Äôs prayer book was found, opened to Psalm 23:4:

‚ÄúEven though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for you are with me; your rod and your staff, they comfort me.‚Äù

On September 30, 1906, memorial services for Hoare were held simultaneously at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral in Hong Kong and Holy Trinity Cathedral in Shanghai. A commemorative stone was later placed in the Anglican Bishop‚Äôs House in Hong Kong in honor of Bishop Hoare.

(2) Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 21‚Äì22; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. b)

(2) ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ Gerald H. LanderÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö21-22; St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d bÔºâ

Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ, 1861‚Äì1934)

Gerald H. Lander served as the fifth Bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1907‚Äì1920). He graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained as a priest in 1885. In 1904, he became a pastor at Liverpool Cathedral, and in 1907, he was consecrated as bishop, succeeding Bishop Joseph C. Hoare, who had perished in the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon.‚Äù

The Church Missionary Society (CMS) sought to revive St. Paul‚Äôs College to provide Western-style education for Chinese students. They proposed relocating the existing seminary to China to attract more funding and students. In 1909, St. Paul‚Äôs College reopened under its original model, with the CMS taking full control of academic affairs. The bishop‚Äôs role became akin to that of a present-day school supervisor.

Beyond the college, Lander witnessed rapid growth in the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. In 1912, the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (the Chinese Anglican Church) was established as an independent province separate from the Church of England. In 1915, St. Paul‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College (now St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College) was founded. During the 1910s, both Diocesan Boys‚Äô School and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School acquired their current campuses, while St. Paul‚Äôs College itself expanded its facilities, doubling its enrollment capacity.

(3) Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ)

(Source: Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 108‚Äì112; Chung Yan Lap, 1975: 7‚Äì11; Zhong Hua Xu Xing Wei Ban Hui, 1921: 269)

(3) ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ Matthew A-JetÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2014aÔºö108-112ÔºõÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1975: 7-11Ôºõ‰∏≠ËèØÁ∫åË°åÂßîËæ¶ÊúÉ1921Ôºö269Ôºâ

Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ, 1840‚Äì1921)

Matthew A-Jet, a pastor, was born on September 3, 1840, in Shiliantang Village, Sanjia Market, Taishan County. In his early years, he worked at mines in Australia, where he became a Christian in Sydney in 1868 and later became a preacher. He returned to China in 1874 and joined Anglican missionaries on itinerant preaching tours in Zengcheng and Beihai. Upon arriving in Hong Kong, he expressed his calling to Bishop John S. Burdon of the Anglican Church and enrolled in the theological program at St. Paul‚Äôs College. At the time, the London Missionary Society (LMS) in Hong Kong lacked Chinese preachers. Upon A-Jet‚Äôs graduation, the LMS borrowed him from the Anglican Church to serve as a missionary. LMS missionaries E. J. Eitel and John Chalmers held A-Jet in high regard.

After serving as an LMS missionary for seven years, A-Jet joined the Anglican Church in 1883. On July 8, 1883, he was ordained as a deacon, becoming the second Chinese cleric ordained by the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. On March 9, 1884, he was ordained as a pastor and succeeded Luo Xin Yuan, who had retired, in leading the ministry of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. After moving to Kowloon in 1902, A-Jet became deeply involved in church work, actively promoting missionary and charitable efforts in Kowloon City and nearby areas. He made significant contributions to the establishment of the Holy Trinity Church parish.

While serving at Holy Trinity Church, A-Jet founded the Almshouse in 1902 to aid the poor, sick, and elderly. He later expanded it to include the Tian Guo Rescue Chapel. In 1903, he joined other Chinese pastors from the LMS and Baptist Church in petitioning the Hong Kong government to allocate land in Kowloon for a Chinese Christian cemetery. The government approved the request in 1904, creating the first Chinese Christian cemetery in Kowloon. In 1906, A-Jet established St. Paul‚Äôs Primary School (later renamed Tian Guo Primary School) in Kowloon Walled City to provide education for impoverished youth, teaching general subjects and vocational skills.

A-Jet also valued literary work and authored several books, including True Doctrine Clarified, Discerning Right from Wrong, and Paths of Heaven. He passed away on January 20, 1921, at the age of 81.

(4) ÁéãË¨ôÂ¶Ç Wong AnangÔºàÊùéÂøóÂâõ2014Ôºö133-136ÔºõÂºµÂøóÂÅâ2005Ôºö143-180Ôºâ

Wong Anang (1847‚Äì1907)

Wong Anang, born in Hong Kong in 1847, hailed from Dongguan, Guangdong. His father, Wong Yuan-shen, was the first preacher of the Rhenish Mission, and his elder brother was Wong Yuk-chu. As a young man, Wong graduated from the Rhenish Mission School and aspired to become a preacher. He studied theology under Ferdinand Genahr and Adam Jrolczyk. Wong graduated from the missionary school in 1866 and was ordained as a pastor in Yongfu in 1884. Over the years, he established churches in nearly ten locations, including:

  • 1877: Sun Wai Church

    • 1879: Fuyong, Dongguan, Xin‚Äôan

      • 1881: Shijia Village

        • 1886: Dongguan

          • 1895: Kaijingbei Church

            • 1896: Tangxia Yeshan

              • 1897: Taiping

                • 1898: Hong Kong

                  • 1902: Shiqi

                    • 1906: Daliang Dalang

                      Wong made significant contributions to the early missionary efforts of the Rhenish Mission.

                      In 1898, while recuperating in Hong Kong, Wong noticed that many believers from Dongguan and Bao‚Äôan had moved to Hong Kong, congregating in the Sai Ying Pun area. He used a missionary‚Äôs residence on Bonham Road West as a venue for Sunday sermons. After recovering, he returned to preach in Xiangshan, Shiqi, Daliang, and Xiaolan. In 1906, he settled in Hong Kong and participated in the planning and construction of the Rhenish Mission Church. Wong passed away in October 1907 at the age of

  1. The church building was completed in 1911, laying the foundation for the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs development in Hong Kong.

    Beyond preaching, Wong deeply reflected on the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture. He moved beyond the ‚ÄúAccommodation Theory‚Äù and ‚ÄúSupplementary Theory,‚Äù striving to demonstrate the superiority of Christianity in all aspects over Confucianism‚Äîa perspective he called the ‚ÄúTranscendence Theory.‚Äù He argued that Christianity was incomparable to Confucianism and firmly believed that Christian faith could lead to national prosperity and strength. Wong contributed articles to The Church News and A Review of the Times. In his later years, he authored works such as Christian and Confucian Monthly Commentary and The Measure of Three Religions.

    (5) Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú Anna M. DeaneÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö69-70ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö89-96Ôºâ

    Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú, ?‚Äì1918)

    Anna M. Deane arrived in Hong Kong in December 1909 as a member of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church from Virginia, USA. After her arrival, she assisted Alfred G. Garr at the ‚ÄúMissionary Home,‚Äù serving as the matron of the women‚Äôs dormitory. In April 1910, she collaborated with Mok Lai Chi to establish the ‚ÄúPentecostal English School,‚Äù where she served as the head teacher.

    In February 1911, Deane felt a calling to evangelize among fishermen. In September 1912, she set up a mission base for fishermen in Shau Kei Wan, founding the ‚ÄúAmerican Pentecostal Mission.‚Äù The mission included a chapel, a school, and a Sunday school. Additionally, she organized waterborne evangelism, regularly taking a small boat to preach the gospel to fishermen.

    In 1915, the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church merged with the Pentecostal Holiness Church, and the Shau Kei Wan chapel became the first Pentecostal Holiness Church in Hong Kong. Deane passed away in Hong Kong in 1918.

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 66‚Äì75; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì99)

    (6) Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Mok Lai ChiÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö66-75ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™2020Ôºö76-99Ôºâ

    Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫, 1867‚Äì1926)

    Mok Lai Chi, a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, was born into a Christian family. His father, Mok Wai Kam, was involved in promoting the self-reliance of To Tsai Church. Mok graduated from Queen‚Äôs College and worked as a civil servant, serving as a clerk at the Observatory, a police interpreter, and the chief translator for the Chinese Secretariat. He was originally a deacon and Sunday school principal at the American Congregational Church. Additionally, he founded Lok Kwan School, where he taught English to over 100 students.

    In 1907, Mok was hired as an interpreter for Alfred G. Garr. On November 10, 1907, he became the second individual to receive Spirit Baptism, making him a pioneer and founding figure of the Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong.

    The church was initially named the ‚ÄúApostolic Faith Church‚Äù but was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Pentecostal Mission‚Äù in 1910. In 1908, Mok launched the first Chinese Pentecostal publication, The Pentecostal Truth, introducing Pentecostal beliefs in Chinese for the first time. He also established the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission as a self-reliant Chinese church, playing a critical role in its growth and development.

    (7) Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 67‚Äì80; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì97)

    (7) ÂÆãÈºéÊñá Sung Teng ManÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö67-80ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö76-97Ôºâ

    Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá, 1883‚Äì1958)

    Sung Teng Man, a second-generation Christian, was baptized during his childhood in Guangzhou and later joined the American Congregational Church in Hong Kong. In 1901, after the completion of the new church building, disputes arose between Chinese members and the missionary society over property rights. Dissatisfied with how the issue was handled, Sung left the church.

    In 1907, when the Pentecostal movement reached Hong Kong, Pastor A. G. Garr and his wife arrived and borrowed the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street for meetings. They emphasized teachings on repentance, forgiveness, and the baptism of the Holy Spirit. Sung, who accompanied his wife to these gatherings, experienced a renewed infilling of the Holy Spirit and became the third believer to receive Spirit Baptism (on November 15, 1907). However, opponents accused the group of heresy and prohibited others from attending their services. In response, Garr, Mok Lai Chi, and Sung Teng Man co-founded the Pentecostal Mission (initially named the Apostolic Faith Church).

    After Mok Lai Chi passed away in 1926, Sung became the leader of the church. In 1939, following his retirement from a senior civil service position, he was ordained as a pastor. Sung guided the church through the three years and eight months of Japanese occupation during World War

  1. He passed away in 1958.

    Following his death, the Kowloon branch of the Pentecostal Mission experienced a split, leading some members to establish the ‚ÄúKowloon Pentecostal Church.‚Äù A legal battle over property rights ensued.

    (8) ÈúçÈùúÂ±± Fok Ching ShanÔºàÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤1996Ôºö327-329ÔºõÈô≥ÁùøÊñá2023Ôºö47-59ÔºõÊííÊØçËÄ≥1919Ôºö 17-18Ôºâ

    Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±, 1851‚Äì1918)

    Fok Ching Shan, born in 1851 in Shunde, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong as a child and later decided to pursue theological studies. He enrolled in St. Paul‚Äôs Theological School under the Anglican Church in Hong Kong, where he remained as a teacher after graduation. Alongside teaching, he assisted with missionary work at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church.

    In 1902, when Pastor Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ) relocated to Kowloon to oversee the ministry at Holy Trinity Church, the need for a parish rector became urgent. At that time, Pastor A-Jet was managing both St. Stephen‚Äôs Church and Holy Trinity Church. In 1903, Fok was ordained as a deacon and became a pastor the following year, succeeding Matthew A-Jet as the rector of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. During his tenure, Fok established a parish council to promote church autonomy and encouraged congregants to contribute financially to achieve self-sufficiency.

    Fok also established a women‚Äôs Bible school within the church to train female evangelists. Beyond St. Stephen‚Äôs Church, he managed All Saints‚Äô Church in Yau Ma Tei and the newly established St. Paul‚Äôs Church. He also founded the Joint Parish Council, where he served as vice-chairman. Fok dedicated 50 years of his life to church service.

    Fok Ching Shan passed away in March 1918 at the age of 67. His passing was deeply mourned by the church community, with thousands of clergy and believers attending his funeral. On March 9, 1919, Bishop Gerald H. Lander of the Diocese of Hong Kong and South China presided over the dedication of a memorial plaque for Fok Ching Shan, recognizing his significant contributions to the church.

    Ôºà9ÔºâÁøÅÊå∫Áîü Yung ParkÔºàÂäâÁëûÊªî1975Ôºö25-32 Ôºâ

    Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü, 1865‚Äì1955)

    Born in Taishan, Guangdong, Yung Park traveled to Hong Kong at the age of 17 before working in the gold mines of San Francisco for four years. He later transitioned to working as a laborer. During this time, he encountered Christianity while studying English at a Gospel Hall and was baptized at the Bethany Chapel in San Francisco in December 1891. The following year, he moved to New York for work and began attending the Congregational Church‚Äôs True Seeking Hall.

    After five years, feeling the need to bring the Gospel to his hometown, Yung raised over 800 gold dollars from fellow believers and returned to Taishan, where he established a Gospel Hall near a temple. In 1903, he moved to Hong Kong and was appointed to the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street. He was ordained as a pastor in 1904.

    In 1912, the American Congregational Church was renamed the ‚ÄúChinese Congregational Church.‚Äù Under Yung‚Äôs leadership, the church established branches in Foshan, Mong Kok, Taishan, Zhongshan, Causeway Bay, and Shau Kei Wan. In 1918, he was elected as the chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council, becoming the first Chinese to hold this position since the council‚Äôs founding in 1915.

    Yung retired in 1947 due to a brain illness and passed away in February 1955 at the age of 91.

Anti-Christian Leaders

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 17‚Äì20; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. a)

(1) ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü Joseph C. HoareÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö17-20Ôºõ St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d aÔºâ

Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü, 1851‚Äì1906)

Joseph C. Hoare was the fourth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1898‚Äì1906). Born in 1851, he graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained into the clergy. Sent by the Church Missionary Society (CMS) to China, Hoare established Trinity Theological School in Ningbo, Zhejiang, where he served as principal. During his 20 years in Ningbo, he became proficient in the Ningbo dialect and developed a unique educational model, ‚Äúevangelical theory through ambulando‚Äù (‚Äúlearning theology through missionary practice‚Äù). This method emphasized hands-on training, with Hoare personally leading students on preaching journeys to remote villages. These missions often lasted up to ten weeks, blending demonstration and instruction. Under his leadership, Trinity Theological School trained dozens of Chinese missionaries, becoming a premier institution in the region.

On June 11, 1898, Hoare was consecrated as the Bishop of Victoria at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral in London and was posted to Hong Kong.

In 1901, Hoare reorganized St. Paul‚Äôs College into a theological seminary focused on training Chinese clergy. He reached an agreement with the CMS, whereby the bishop directly managed the teaching while the CMS provided additional staff and resources. During his tenure, the Chinese Anglican community in Hong Kong expanded, adding Holy Trinity Church and All Saints‚Äô Church alongside St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. Hoare also established the Chinese Anglican Church Body in 1902, which was incorporated as a legal entity under his leadership as chairman.

In September 1906, Hoare led four theology students from St. Paul‚Äôs College (Ho Dao-pei, Cheng Yung-chi, Leung Kam-po, and Wong Ting-sang) on a missionary trip to Castle Peak by sea. On their return journey on September 18, they encountered the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon,‚Äù which capsized their boat. Both Hoare and the students perished, and their bodies were never recovered. Only Hoare‚Äôs prayer book was found, opened to Psalm 23:4:

‚ÄúEven though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for you are with me; your rod and your staff, they comfort me.‚Äù

On September 30, 1906, memorial services for Hoare were held simultaneously at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral in Hong Kong and Holy Trinity Cathedral in Shanghai. A commemorative stone was later placed in the Anglican Bishop‚Äôs House in Hong Kong in honor of Bishop Hoare.

Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ, 1861‚Äì1934)

Gerald H. Lander served as the fifth Bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1907‚Äì1920). He graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained as a priest in 1885. In 1904, he became a pastor at Liverpool Cathedral, and in 1907, he was consecrated as bishop, succeeding Bishop Joseph C. Hoare, who had perished in the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon.‚Äù

The Church Missionary Society (CMS) sought to revive St. Paul‚Äôs College to provide Western-style education for Chinese students. They proposed relocating the existing seminary to China to attract more funding and students. In 1909, St. Paul‚Äôs College reopened under its original model, with the CMS taking full control of academic affairs. The bishop‚Äôs role became akin to that of a present-day school supervisor.

Beyond the college, Lander witnessed rapid growth in the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. In 1912, the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (the Chinese Anglican Church) was established as an independent province separate from the Church of England. In 1915, St. Paul‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College (now St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College) was founded. During the 1910s, both Diocesan Boys‚Äô School and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School acquired their current campuses, while St. Paul‚Äôs College itself expanded its facilities, doubling its enrollment capacity.

(3) ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ Matthew A-JetÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2014aÔºö108-112ÔºõÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1975: 7-11Ôºõ‰∏≠ËèØÁ∫åË°åÂßîËæ¶ÊúÉ1921Ôºö269Ôºâ

Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ, 1840‚Äì1921)

Matthew A-Jet, a pastor, was born on September 3, 1840, in Shiliantang Village, Sanjia Market, Taishan County. In his early years, he worked at mines in Australia, where he became a Christian in Sydney in 1868 and later became a preacher. He returned to China in 1874 and joined Anglican missionaries on itinerant preaching tours in Zengcheng and Beihai. Upon arriving in Hong Kong, he expressed his calling to Bishop John S. Burdon of the Anglican Church and enrolled in the theological program at St. Paul‚Äôs College. At the time, the London Missionary Society (LMS) in Hong Kong lacked Chinese preachers. Upon A-Jet‚Äôs graduation, the LMS borrowed him from the Anglican Church to serve as a missionary. LMS missionaries E. J. Eitel and John Chalmers held A-Jet in high regard.

After serving as an LMS missionary for seven years, A-Jet joined the Anglican Church in 1883. On July 8, 1883, he was ordained as a deacon, becoming the second Chinese cleric ordained by the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. On March 9, 1884, he was ordained as a pastor and succeeded Luo Xin Yuan, who had retired, in leading the ministry of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. After moving to Kowloon in 1902, A-Jet became deeply involved in church work, actively promoting missionary and charitable efforts in Kowloon City and nearby areas. He made significant contributions to the establishment of the Holy Trinity Church parish.

While serving at Holy Trinity Church, A-Jet founded the Almshouse in 1902 to aid the poor, sick, and elderly. He later expanded it to include the Tian Guo Rescue Chapel. In 1903, he joined other Chinese pastors from the LMS and Baptist Church in petitioning the Hong Kong government to allocate land in Kowloon for a Chinese Christian cemetery. The government approved the request in 1904, creating the first Chinese Christian cemetery in Kowloon. In 1906, A-Jet established St. Paul‚Äôs Primary School (later renamed Tian Guo Primary School) in Kowloon Walled City to provide education for impoverished youth, teaching general subjects and vocational skills.

A-Jet also valued literary work and authored several books, including True Doctrine Clarified, Discerning Right from Wrong, and Paths of Heaven. He passed away on January 20, 1921, at the age of 81.

(4) ÁéãË¨ôÂ¶Ç Wong AnangÔºàÊùéÂøóÂâõ2014Ôºö133-136ÔºõÂºµÂøóÂÅâ2005Ôºö143-180Ôºâ

Wong Anang (1847‚Äì1907)

Wong Anang, born in Hong Kong in 1847, hailed from Dongguan, Guangdong. His father, Wong Yuan-shen, was the first preacher of the Rhenish Mission, and his elder brother was Wong Yuk-chu. As a young man, Wong graduated from the Rhenish Mission School and aspired to become a preacher. He studied theology under Ferdinand Genahr and Adam Jrolczyk. Wong graduated from the missionary school in 1866 and was ordained as a pastor in Yongfu in 1884. Over the years, he established churches in nearly ten locations, including:

  • 1877: Sun Wai Church

    • 1879: Fuyong, Dongguan, Xin‚Äôan

      • 1881: Shijia Village

        • 1886: Dongguan

          • 1895: Kaijingbei Church

            • 1896: Tangxia Yeshan

              • 1897: Taiping

                • 1898: Hong Kong

                  • 1902: Shiqi

                    • 1906: Daliang Dalang

                      Wong made significant contributions to the early missionary efforts of the Rhenish Mission.

                      In 1898, while recuperating in Hong Kong, Wong noticed that many believers from Dongguan and Bao‚Äôan had moved to Hong Kong, congregating in the Sai Ying Pun area. He used a missionary‚Äôs residence on Bonham Road West as a venue for Sunday sermons. After recovering, he returned to preach in Xiangshan, Shiqi, Daliang, and Xiaolan. In 1906, he settled in Hong Kong and participated in the planning and construction of the Rhenish Mission Church. Wong passed away in October 1907 at the age of

  1. The church building was completed in 1911, laying the foundation for the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs development in Hong Kong.

    Beyond preaching, Wong deeply reflected on the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture. He moved beyond the ‚ÄúAccommodation Theory‚Äù and ‚ÄúSupplementary Theory,‚Äù striving to demonstrate the superiority of Christianity in all aspects over Confucianism‚Äîa perspective he called the ‚ÄúTranscendence Theory.‚Äù He argued that Christianity was incomparable to Confucianism and firmly believed that Christian faith could lead to national prosperity and strength. Wong contributed articles to The Church News and A Review of the Times. In his later years, he authored works such as Christian and Confucian Monthly Commentary and The Measure of Three Religions.

    ÁéãË¨ôÂ¶ÇÔºà1847-1907ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±Êù±Ëéû‰∫∫Ôºå1847Âπ¥ÁîüÊñºÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÔºåÁà∂Ë¶™ÊòØÁ¶ÆË≥¢ÊúÉÈ¶ñ‰ΩçÂÇ≥ÈÅìÁéãÂÖÉÊ∑±ÔºåÂÖÑÈï∑ÁéãÁÖúÂàù„ÄÇÂ∞ëÂπ¥Âú®Á¶ÆË≥¢ÊúÉÂ≠∏Â†ÇÁï¢Ê•≠ÔºåÂæåÁ´ãÂøóÂÇ≥ÈÅìÔºåÈö®ËëâÁ¥çÊ∏ÖÔºàFerdinand GenahrÔºâÂèäÂÖ¨Â≠´ÊÉ†ÔºàAdam JrolczykÔºâÁøíÁ•ûÁßë„ÄÇ1866Âπ¥ÂÇ≥ÊïôÂ≠∏Ê†°Áï¢Ê•≠Ôºå1884Âπ¥ÊñºÊ∞∏Á¶èÂèóÊåâÁ´ãÁÇ∫ÁâßÂ∏´„ÄÇÂÖàÂæåÊñºËøëÂçÅËôïÂú∞ÊñπÂª∫Á´ãÊïôÊúÉÔºà1877Âπ¥ÔºöÊñ∞ÂúçËÅñÊúÉÔºõ 1879Âπ¥ÔºöÁ¶èÊ∞∏„ÄÅÊù±Ëéû„ÄÅÊñ∞ÂÆâÔºõ1881Âπ¥ÔºöÁü≥ÂÆ∂ÊùëÔºõ1886Âπ¥ÔºöÊù±ËéûÔºõ1895 Âπ¥ÔºöÈñãÈÄïËÉåËÅñÊúÉÔºõ1896Âπ¥ÔºöÂ°òÂªàËÄ∂Â±±Ôºõ1897Âπ¥ÔºöÂ§™Âπ≥Ôºõ1898Âπ¥ÔºöÈ¶ôÊ∏ØÔºõ1902Âπ¥ÔºöÁü≥Ê≠ßÔºõ1906Âπ¥ÔºöÂ§ßËâØÂ§ßÊ¨ñÔºâ ÔºåÂ∞çÁ¶ÆË≥¢ÊúÉÊó©ÊúüÂÇ≥ÈÅì‰∫ãÂ∑•Ôºå‰ΩúÂá∫ÈáçÂ§ßË≤¢Áçª„ÄÇ

    (5) Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 69‚Äì70; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 89‚Äì96)

    (5) Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú Anna M. DeaneÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö69-70ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö89-96Ôºâ

    Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú, ?‚Äì1918)

    Anna M. Deane arrived in Hong Kong in December 1909 as a member of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church from Virginia, USA. After her arrival, she assisted Alfred G. Garr at the ‚ÄúMissionary Home,‚Äù serving as the matron of the women‚Äôs dormitory. In April 1910, she collaborated with Mok Lai Chi to establish the ‚ÄúPentecostal English School,‚Äù where she served as the head teacher.

    In February 1911, Deane felt a calling to evangelize among fishermen. In September 1912, she set up a mission base for fishermen in Shau Kei Wan, founding the ‚ÄúAmerican Pentecostal Mission.‚Äù The mission included a chapel, a school, and a Sunday school. Additionally, she organized waterborne evangelism, regularly taking a small boat to preach the gospel to fishermen.

    In 1915, the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church merged with the Pentecostal Holiness Church, and the Shau Kei Wan chapel became the first Pentecostal Holiness Church in Hong Kong. Deane passed away in Hong Kong in 1918.

    (6) Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 66‚Äì75; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì99)

    (6) Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Mok Lai ChiÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö66-75ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™2020Ôºö76-99Ôºâ

    Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫, 1867‚Äì1926)

    Mok Lai Chi, a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, was born into a Christian family. His father, Mok Wai Kam, was involved in promoting the self-reliance of To Tsai Church. Mok graduated from Queen‚Äôs College and worked as a civil servant, serving as a clerk at the Observatory, a police interpreter, and the chief translator for the Chinese Secretariat. He was originally a deacon and Sunday school principal at the American Congregational Church. Additionally, he founded Lok Kwan School, where he taught English to over 100 students.

    In 1907, Mok was hired as an interpreter for Alfred G. Garr. On November 10, 1907, he became the second individual to receive Spirit Baptism, making him a pioneer and founding figure of the Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong.

    The church was initially named the ‚ÄúApostolic Faith Church‚Äù but was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Pentecostal Mission‚Äù in 1910. In 1908, Mok launched the first Chinese Pentecostal publication, The Pentecostal Truth, introducing Pentecostal beliefs in Chinese for the first time. He also established the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission as a self-reliant Chinese church, playing a critical role in its growth and development.

    Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Ôºà1867‚Äì1926ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±Êñ∞ÂÆâ‰∫∫ÔºåÁîüÊñºÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆ∂Â∫≠ÔºåÁà∂Ë¶™Ëé´ÊÉ†Èå¶ÊõæÂ∫≠ÊõæÂèÉËàáÊé®ÂãïÈÅìÊøüÊúÉÂ†ÇÁöÑËá™Á´ã„ÄÇÁï¢Ê•≠ÊñºÁöá‰ªÅÊõ∏Èô¢Ôºå‰ªªËÅ∑ÊîøÂ∫úÂÖ¨ÂãôÂì°ÔºàÂ§©ÊñáÂè∞Êõ∏Ë®ò„ÄÅË≠¶Â±¨ÈÄöË≠Ø„ÄÅËèØÊ∞ëÊîøÂãôÂè∏Á∏ΩÁøªË≠ØÔºâ„ÄÇÂéüÁÇ∫ÁæéËèØËá™ÁêÜÊúÉÂü∑‰∫ãÂèä‰∏ªÊó•Â≠∏Ê†°Èï∑„ÄÇÈñãË®≠Ê®ÇÁæ§Êõ∏Â°æÔºåÊïôÊéàËã±ÊñáÔºåÂ≠∏ÁîüÂ§öÈÅîÁôæ‰∫∫„ÄÇ1907Âπ¥ÔºåÂõ†ÂèóËÅòÁÇ∫ÂòâÊ¥ªÂäõÔºàAlfred

    ÊïôÊúÉÂàùÊúüÁ®±„Äå‰ΩøÂæí‰ø°ÂøÉÊúÉ„ÄçÔºå1910Âπ¥ÊîπÂêç„ÄåÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊúÉ„Äç„ÄÇ1908Âπ¥ÂâµËæ¶È¶ñ‰ªΩ‰∏≠Êñá‰∫îÊó¨ÂÆóÂ†±Âàä„Ää‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÁúüÁêÜÂ†±„ÄãÔºåÈ¶ñÊ¨°Â∞á‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄ‰ø°‰ª∞‰ª•‰∏≠ÊñáÂëàÁèæ„ÄÇÂêåÊôÇÔºå‰ªñÂèàÂ∞áÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊúÉÁïåÂÆöÁÇ∫ËèØ‰∫∫Ëá™Á´ãÊïôÊúÉÔºåÂ∞çÊñºÈ¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊúÉÁöÑÁôºÂ±ïÔºåÊâÆÊºîËàâË∂≥ËºïÈáçÁöÑËßíËâ≤„ÄÇ

    (7) Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 67‚Äì80; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì97)

    (7) ÂÆãÈºéÊñá Sung Teng ManÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö67-80ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö76-97Ôºâ

    Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá, 1883‚Äì1958)

    Sung Teng Man, a second-generation Christian, was baptized during his childhood in Guangzhou and later joined the American Congregational Church in Hong Kong. In 1901, after the completion of the new church building, disputes arose between Chinese members and the missionary society over property rights. Dissatisfied with how the issue was handled, Sung left the church.

    In 1907, when the Pentecostal movement reached Hong Kong, Pastor A. G. Garr and his wife arrived and borrowed the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street for meetings. They emphasized teachings on repentance, forgiveness, and the baptism of the Holy Spirit. Sung, who accompanied his wife to these gatherings, experienced a renewed infilling of the Holy Spirit and became the third believer to receive Spirit Baptism (on November 15, 1907). However, opponents accused the group of heresy and prohibited others from attending their services. In response, Garr, Mok Lai Chi, and Sung Teng Man co-founded the Pentecostal Mission (initially named the Apostolic Faith Church).

    After Mok Lai Chi passed away in 1926, Sung became the leader of the church. In 1939, following his retirement from a senior civil service position, he was ordained as a pastor. Sung guided the church through the three years and eight months of Japanese occupation during World War

  1. He passed away in 1958.

    Following his death, the Kowloon branch of the Pentecostal Mission experienced a split, leading some members to establish the ‚ÄúKowloon Pentecostal Church.‚Äù A legal battle over property rights ensued.

    1926Âπ¥ÔºåËé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Èõ¢‰∏ñÂæåÔºåÂÆãÊàêÁÇ∫ÊïôÊúÉÈ†òÂ∞é‰∫∫„ÄÇ1939Âπ¥Ëá™ÊîøÂ∫úÈ´òÁ¥öÂÖ¨ÂãôÂì°ÈÄÄ‰ºëÂæåÔºåË¢´ÊåâÁ´ãÁÇ∫ÁâßÂ∏´„ÄÇÂÆãÂ∏∂È†òÊïôÊúÉÁ∂ìÊ≠∑Êó•Ê≤ª‰∏âÂπ¥Èõ∂ÂÖ´ÂÄãÊúàÔºåËá≥1958Âπ¥Èõ¢‰∏ñ„ÄÇÂÆãÈõ¢‰∏ñÂæåÔºå‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊúÉ‰πùÈæçÊîØÂ†ÇÂá∫ÁèæÂàÜË£ÇÔºå‰ø°ÂæíÂè¶Á´ã„Äå‰πùÈæç‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊúÉ„ÄçÔºå‰∏¶Â∞±Áî¢Ê¨äÂïèÈ°åÂ∞çËñÑÂÖ¨Â†Ç„ÄÇ

    (8) Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±)

    (Source: Liu Yue Sheng, 1996: 327‚Äì329; Chen Rui Wen, 2023: 47‚Äì59; Samuel, 1919: 17‚Äì18)

    (8) ÈúçÈùúÂ±± Fok Ching ShanÔºàÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤1996Ôºö327-329ÔºõÈô≥ÁùøÊñá2023Ôºö47-59ÔºõÊííÊØçËÄ≥1919Ôºö 17-18Ôºâ

    Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±, 1851‚Äì1918)

    Fok Ching Shan, born in 1851 in Shunde, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong as a child and later decided to pursue theological studies. He enrolled in St. Paul‚Äôs Theological School under the Anglican Church in Hong Kong, where he remained as a teacher after graduation. Alongside teaching, he assisted with missionary work at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church.

    In 1902, when Pastor Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ) relocated to Kowloon to oversee the ministry at Holy Trinity Church, the need for a parish rector became urgent. At that time, Pastor A-Jet was managing both St. Stephen‚Äôs Church and Holy Trinity Church. In 1903, Fok was ordained as a deacon and became a pastor the following year, succeeding Matthew A-Jet as the rector of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. During his tenure, Fok established a parish council to promote church autonomy and encouraged congregants to contribute financially to achieve self-sufficiency.

    Fok also established a women‚Äôs Bible school within the church to train female evangelists. Beyond St. Stephen‚Äôs Church, he managed All Saints‚Äô Church in Yau Ma Tei and the newly established St. Paul‚Äôs Church. He also founded the Joint Parish Council, where he served as vice-chairman. Fok dedicated 50 years of his life to church service.

    Fok Ching Shan passed away in March 1918 at the age of 67. His passing was deeply mourned by the church community, with thousands of clergy and believers attending his funeral. On March 9, 1919, Bishop Gerald H. Lander of the Diocese of Hong Kong and South China presided over the dedication of a memorial plaque for Fok Ching Shan, recognizing his significant contributions to the church.

    (9) Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü)

    (Source: Liu Rui Tao, 1975: 25‚Äì32)

    Ôºà9ÔºâÁøÅÊå∫Áîü Yung ParkÔºàÂäâÁëûÊªî1975Ôºö25-32 Ôºâ

    Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü, 1865‚Äì1955)

    Born in Taishan, Guangdong, Yung Park traveled to Hong Kong at the age of 17 before working in the gold mines of San Francisco for four years. He later transitioned to working as a laborer. During this time, he encountered Christianity while studying English at a Gospel Hall and was baptized at the Bethany Chapel in San Francisco in December 1891. The following year, he moved to New York for work and began attending the Congregational Church‚Äôs True Seeking Hall.

    After five years, feeling the need to bring the Gospel to his hometown, Yung raised over 800 gold dollars from fellow believers and returned to Taishan, where he established a Gospel Hall near a temple. In 1903, he moved to Hong Kong and was appointed to the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street. He was ordained as a pastor in 1904.

    In 1912, the American Congregational Church was renamed the ‚ÄúChinese Congregational Church.‚Äù Under Yung‚Äôs leadership, the church established branches in Foshan, Mong Kok, Taishan, Zhongshan, Causeway Bay, and Shau Kei Wan. In 1918, he was elected as the chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council, becoming the first Chinese to hold this position since the council‚Äôs founding in 1915.

    Yung retired in 1947 due to a brain illness and passed away in February 1955 at the age of 91.

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 17‚Äì20; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. a)

(1) ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü Joseph C. HoareÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö17-20Ôºõ St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d aÔºâ

Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü, 1851‚Äì1906)

Joseph C. Hoare was the fourth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1898‚Äì1906). Born in 1851, he graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained into the clergy. Sent by the Church Missionary Society (CMS) to China, Hoare established Trinity Theological School in Ningbo, Zhejiang, where he served as principal. During his 20 years in Ningbo, he became proficient in the Ningbo dialect and developed a unique educational model, ‚Äúevangelical theory through ambulando‚Äù (‚Äúlearning theology through missionary practice‚Äù). This method emphasized hands-on training, with Hoare personally leading students on preaching journeys to remote villages. These missions often lasted up to ten weeks, blending demonstration and instruction. Under his leadership, Trinity Theological School trained dozens of Chinese missionaries, becoming a premier institution in the region.

On June 11, 1898, Hoare was consecrated as the Bishop of Victoria at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral in London and was posted to Hong Kong.

In 1901, Hoare reorganized St. Paul‚Äôs College into a theological seminary focused on training Chinese clergy. He reached an agreement with the CMS, whereby the bishop directly managed the teaching while the CMS provided additional staff and resources. During his tenure, the Chinese Anglican community in Hong Kong expanded, adding Holy Trinity Church and All Saints‚Äô Church alongside St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. Hoare also established the Chinese Anglican Church Body in 1902, which was incorporated as a legal entity under his leadership as chairman.

In September 1906, Hoare led four theology students from St. Paul‚Äôs College (Ho Dao-pei, Cheng Yung-chi, Leung Kam-po, and Wong Ting-sang) on a missionary trip to Castle Peak by sea. On their return journey on September 18, they encountered the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon,‚Äù which capsized their boat. Both Hoare and the students perished, and their bodies were never recovered. Only Hoare‚Äôs prayer book was found, opened to Psalm 23:4:

‚ÄúEven though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for you are with me; your rod and your staff, they comfort me.‚Äù

On September 30, 1906, memorial services for Hoare were held simultaneously at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral in Hong Kong and Holy Trinity Cathedral in Shanghai. A commemorative stone was later placed in the Anglican Bishop‚Äôs House in Hong Kong in honor of Bishop Hoare.

(2) Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 21‚Äì22; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. b)

(2) ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ Gerald H. LanderÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö21-22; St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d bÔºâ

Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ, 1861‚Äì1934)

Gerald H. Lander served as the fifth Bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1907‚Äì1920). He graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained as a priest in 1885. In 1904, he became a pastor at Liverpool Cathedral, and in 1907, he was consecrated as bishop, succeeding Bishop Joseph C. Hoare, who had perished in the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon.‚Äù

The Church Missionary Society (CMS) sought to revive St. Paul‚Äôs College to provide Western-style education for Chinese students. They proposed relocating the existing seminary to China to attract more funding and students. In 1909, St. Paul‚Äôs College reopened under its original model, with the CMS taking full control of academic affairs. The bishop‚Äôs role became akin to that of a present-day school supervisor.

Beyond the college, Lander witnessed rapid growth in the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. In 1912, the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (the Chinese Anglican Church) was established as an independent province separate from the Church of England. In 1915, St. Paul‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College (now St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College) was founded. During the 1910s, both Diocesan Boys‚Äô School and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School acquired their current campuses, while St. Paul‚Äôs College itself expanded its facilities, doubling its enrollment capacity.

(3) Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ)

(3) ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ Matthew A-JetÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2014aÔºö108-112ÔºõÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1975: 7-11Ôºõ‰∏≠ËèØÁ∫åË°åÂßîËæ¶ÊúÉ1921Ôºö269Ôºâ

Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ, 1840‚Äì1921)

Matthew A-Jet, a pastor, was born on September 3, 1840, in Shiliantang Village, Sanjia Market, Taishan County. In his early years, he worked at mines in Australia, where he became a Christian in Sydney in 1868 and later became a preacher. He returned to China in 1874 and joined Anglican missionaries on itinerant preaching tours in Zengcheng and Beihai. Upon arriving in Hong Kong, he expressed his calling to Bishop John S. Burdon of the Anglican Church and enrolled in the theological program at St. Paul‚Äôs College. At the time, the London Missionary Society (LMS) in Hong Kong lacked Chinese preachers. Upon A-Jet‚Äôs graduation, the LMS borrowed him from the Anglican Church to serve as a missionary. LMS missionaries E. J. Eitel and John Chalmers held A-Jet in high regard.

After serving as an LMS missionary for seven years, A-Jet joined the Anglican Church in 1883. On July 8, 1883, he was ordained as a deacon, becoming the second Chinese cleric ordained by the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. On March 9, 1884, he was ordained as a pastor and succeeded Luo Xin Yuan, who had retired, in leading the ministry of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. After moving to Kowloon in 1902, A-Jet became deeply involved in church work, actively promoting missionary and charitable efforts in Kowloon City and nearby areas. He made significant contributions to the establishment of the Holy Trinity Church parish.

While serving at Holy Trinity Church, A-Jet founded the Almshouse in 1902 to aid the poor, sick, and elderly. He later expanded it to include the Tian Guo Rescue Chapel. In 1903, he joined other Chinese pastors from the LMS and Baptist Church in petitioning the Hong Kong government to allocate land in Kowloon for a Chinese Christian cemetery. The government approved the request in 1904, creating the first Chinese Christian cemetery in Kowloon. In 1906, A-Jet established St. Paul‚Äôs Primary School (later renamed Tian Guo Primary School) in Kowloon Walled City to provide education for impoverished youth, teaching general subjects and vocational skills.

A-Jet also valued literary work and authored several books, including True Doctrine Clarified, Discerning Right from Wrong, and Paths of Heaven. He passed away on January 20, 1921, at the age of 81.

Wong Anang (1847‚Äì1907)

Wong Anang, born in Hong Kong in 1847, hailed from Dongguan, Guangdong. His father, Wong Yuan-shen, was the first preacher of the Rhenish Mission, and his elder brother was Wong Yuk-chu. As a young man, Wong graduated from the Rhenish Mission School and aspired to become a preacher. He studied theology under Ferdinand Genahr and Adam Jrolczyk. Wong graduated from the missionary school in 1866 and was ordained as a pastor in Yongfu in 1884. Over the years, he established churches in nearly ten locations, including:

  • 1877: Sun Wai Church

    • 1879: Fuyong, Dongguan, Xin‚Äôan

      • 1881: Shijia Village

        • 1886: Dongguan

          • 1895: Kaijingbei Church

            • 1896: Tangxia Yeshan

              • 1897: Taiping

                • 1898: Hong Kong

                  • 1902: Shiqi

                    • 1906: Daliang Dalang

                      Wong made significant contributions to the early missionary efforts of the Rhenish Mission.

                      In 1898, while recuperating in Hong Kong, Wong noticed that many believers from Dongguan and Bao‚Äôan had moved to Hong Kong, congregating in the Sai Ying Pun area. He used a missionary‚Äôs residence on Bonham Road West as a venue for Sunday sermons. After recovering, he returned to preach in Xiangshan, Shiqi, Daliang, and Xiaolan. In 1906, he settled in Hong Kong and participated in the planning and construction of the Rhenish Mission Church. Wong passed away in October 1907 at the age of

  1. The church building was completed in 1911, laying the foundation for the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs development in Hong Kong.

    Beyond preaching, Wong deeply reflected on the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture. He moved beyond the ‚ÄúAccommodation Theory‚Äù and ‚ÄúSupplementary Theory,‚Äù striving to demonstrate the superiority of Christianity in all aspects over Confucianism‚Äîa perspective he called the ‚ÄúTranscendence Theory.‚Äù He argued that Christianity was incomparable to Confucianism and firmly believed that Christian faith could lead to national prosperity and strength. Wong contributed articles to The Church News and A Review of the Times. In his later years, he authored works such as Christian and Confucian Monthly Commentary and The Measure of Three Religions.

    (5) Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú Anna M. DeaneÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö69-70ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö89-96Ôºâ

    Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú, ?‚Äì1918)

    Anna M. Deane arrived in Hong Kong in December 1909 as a member of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church from Virginia, USA. After her arrival, she assisted Alfred G. Garr at the ‚ÄúMissionary Home,‚Äù serving as the matron of the women‚Äôs dormitory. In April 1910, she collaborated with Mok Lai Chi to establish the ‚ÄúPentecostal English School,‚Äù where she served as the head teacher.

    In February 1911, Deane felt a calling to evangelize among fishermen. In September 1912, she set up a mission base for fishermen in Shau Kei Wan, founding the ‚ÄúAmerican Pentecostal Mission.‚Äù The mission included a chapel, a school, and a Sunday school. Additionally, she organized waterborne evangelism, regularly taking a small boat to preach the gospel to fishermen.

    In 1915, the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church merged with the Pentecostal Holiness Church, and the Shau Kei Wan chapel became the first Pentecostal Holiness Church in Hong Kong. Deane passed away in Hong Kong in 1918.

    Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫, 1867‚Äì1926)

    Mok Lai Chi, a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, was born into a Christian family. His father, Mok Wai Kam, was involved in promoting the self-reliance of To Tsai Church. Mok graduated from Queen‚Äôs College and worked as a civil servant, serving as a clerk at the Observatory, a police interpreter, and the chief translator for the Chinese Secretariat. He was originally a deacon and Sunday school principal at the American Congregational Church. Additionally, he founded Lok Kwan School, where he taught English to over 100 students.

    In 1907, Mok was hired as an interpreter for Alfred G. Garr. On November 10, 1907, he became the second individual to receive Spirit Baptism, making him a pioneer and founding figure of the Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong.

    The church was initially named the ‚ÄúApostolic Faith Church‚Äù but was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Pentecostal Mission‚Äù in 1910. In 1908, Mok launched the first Chinese Pentecostal publication, The Pentecostal Truth, introducing Pentecostal beliefs in Chinese for the first time. He also established the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission as a self-reliant Chinese church, playing a critical role in its growth and development.

    Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Ôºà1867‚Äì1926ÔºâÔºåÂª£Êù±Êñ∞ÂÆâ‰∫∫ÔºåÁîüÊñºÂü∫Áù£ÊïôÂÆ∂Â∫≠ÔºåÁà∂Ë¶™Ëé´ÊÉ†Èå¶ÊõæÂ∫≠ÊõæÂèÉËàáÊé®ÂãïÈÅìÊøüÊúÉÂ†ÇÁöÑËá™Á´ã„ÄÇÁï¢Ê•≠ÊñºÁöá‰ªÅÊõ∏Èô¢Ôºå‰ªªËÅ∑ÊîøÂ∫úÂÖ¨ÂãôÂì°ÔºàÂ§©ÊñáÂè∞Êõ∏Ë®ò„ÄÅË≠¶Â±¨ÈÄöË≠Ø„ÄÅËèØÊ∞ëÊîøÂãôÂè∏Á∏ΩÁøªË≠ØÔºâ„ÄÇÂéüÁÇ∫ÁæéËèØËá™ÁêÜÊúÉÂü∑‰∫ãÂèä‰∏ªÊó•Â≠∏Ê†°Èï∑„ÄÇÈñãË®≠Ê®ÇÁæ§Êõ∏Â°æÔºåÊïôÊéàËã±ÊñáÔºåÂ≠∏ÁîüÂ§öÈÅîÁôæ‰∫∫„ÄÇ1907Âπ¥ÔºåÂõ†ÂèóËÅòÁÇ∫ÂòâÊ¥ªÂäõÔºàAlfred

    (7) Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 67‚Äì80; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì97)

    (7) ÂÆãÈºéÊñá Sung Teng ManÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö67-80ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö76-97Ôºâ

    Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá, 1883‚Äì1958)

    Sung Teng Man, a second-generation Christian, was baptized during his childhood in Guangzhou and later joined the American Congregational Church in Hong Kong. In 1901, after the completion of the new church building, disputes arose between Chinese members and the missionary society over property rights. Dissatisfied with how the issue was handled, Sung left the church.

    In 1907, when the Pentecostal movement reached Hong Kong, Pastor A. G. Garr and his wife arrived and borrowed the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street for meetings. They emphasized teachings on repentance, forgiveness, and the baptism of the Holy Spirit. Sung, who accompanied his wife to these gatherings, experienced a renewed infilling of the Holy Spirit and became the third believer to receive Spirit Baptism (on November 15, 1907). However, opponents accused the group of heresy and prohibited others from attending their services. In response, Garr, Mok Lai Chi, and Sung Teng Man co-founded the Pentecostal Mission (initially named the Apostolic Faith Church).

    After Mok Lai Chi passed away in 1926, Sung became the leader of the church. In 1939, following his retirement from a senior civil service position, he was ordained as a pastor. Sung guided the church through the three years and eight months of Japanese occupation during World War

  1. He passed away in 1958.

    Following his death, the Kowloon branch of the Pentecostal Mission experienced a split, leading some members to establish the ‚ÄúKowloon Pentecostal Church.‚Äù A legal battle over property rights ensued.

    (8) ÈúçÈùúÂ±± Fok Ching ShanÔºàÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤1996Ôºö327-329ÔºõÈô≥ÁùøÊñá2023Ôºö47-59ÔºõÊííÊØçËÄ≥1919Ôºö 17-18Ôºâ

    Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±, 1851‚Äì1918)

    Fok Ching Shan, born in 1851 in Shunde, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong as a child and later decided to pursue theological studies. He enrolled in St. Paul‚Äôs Theological School under the Anglican Church in Hong Kong, where he remained as a teacher after graduation. Alongside teaching, he assisted with missionary work at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church.

    In 1902, when Pastor Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ) relocated to Kowloon to oversee the ministry at Holy Trinity Church, the need for a parish rector became urgent. At that time, Pastor A-Jet was managing both St. Stephen‚Äôs Church and Holy Trinity Church. In 1903, Fok was ordained as a deacon and became a pastor the following year, succeeding Matthew A-Jet as the rector of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. During his tenure, Fok established a parish council to promote church autonomy and encouraged congregants to contribute financially to achieve self-sufficiency.

    Fok also established a women‚Äôs Bible school within the church to train female evangelists. Beyond St. Stephen‚Äôs Church, he managed All Saints‚Äô Church in Yau Ma Tei and the newly established St. Paul‚Äôs Church. He also founded the Joint Parish Council, where he served as vice-chairman. Fok dedicated 50 years of his life to church service.

    Fok Ching Shan passed away in March 1918 at the age of 67. His passing was deeply mourned by the church community, with thousands of clergy and believers attending his funeral. On March 9, 1919, Bishop Gerald H. Lander of the Diocese of Hong Kong and South China presided over the dedication of a memorial plaque for Fok Ching Shan, recognizing his significant contributions to the church.

    Ôºà9ÔºâÁøÅÊå∫Áîü Yung ParkÔºàÂäâÁëûÊªî1975Ôºö25-32 Ôºâ

    Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü, 1865‚Äì1955)

    Born in Taishan, Guangdong, Yung Park traveled to Hong Kong at the age of 17 before working in the gold mines of San Francisco for four years. He later transitioned to working as a laborer. During this time, he encountered Christianity while studying English at a Gospel Hall and was baptized at the Bethany Chapel in San Francisco in December 1891. The following year, he moved to New York for work and began attending the Congregational Church‚Äôs True Seeking Hall.

    After five years, feeling the need to bring the Gospel to his hometown, Yung raised over 800 gold dollars from fellow believers and returned to Taishan, where he established a Gospel Hall near a temple. In 1903, he moved to Hong Kong and was appointed to the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street. He was ordained as a pastor in 1904.

    In 1912, the American Congregational Church was renamed the ‚ÄúChinese Congregational Church.‚Äù Under Yung‚Äôs leadership, the church established branches in Foshan, Mong Kok, Taishan, Zhongshan, Causeway Bay, and Shau Kei Wan. In 1918, he was elected as the chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council, becoming the first Chinese to hold this position since the council‚Äôs founding in 1915.

    Yung retired in 1947 due to a brain illness and passed away in February 1955 at the age of 91.

Anti-Christian Events/Movements

(1) Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 17‚Äì20; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. a)

(1) ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü Joseph C. HoareÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö17-20Ôºõ St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d aÔºâ

Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü, 1851‚Äì1906)

Joseph C. Hoare was the fourth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1898‚Äì1906). Born in 1851, he graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained into the clergy. Sent by the Church Missionary Society (CMS) to China, Hoare established Trinity Theological School in Ningbo, Zhejiang, where he served as principal. During his 20 years in Ningbo, he became proficient in the Ningbo dialect and developed a unique educational model, ‚Äúevangelical theory through ambulando‚Äù (‚Äúlearning theology through missionary practice‚Äù). This method emphasized hands-on training, with Hoare personally leading students on preaching journeys to remote villages. These missions often lasted up to ten weeks, blending demonstration and instruction. Under his leadership, Trinity Theological School trained dozens of Chinese missionaries, becoming a premier institution in the region.

On June 11, 1898, Hoare was consecrated as the Bishop of Victoria at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral in London and was posted to Hong Kong.

In 1901, Hoare reorganized St. Paul‚Äôs College into a theological seminary focused on training Chinese clergy. He reached an agreement with the CMS, whereby the bishop directly managed the teaching while the CMS provided additional staff and resources. During his tenure, the Chinese Anglican community in Hong Kong expanded, adding Holy Trinity Church and All Saints‚Äô Church alongside St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. Hoare also established the Chinese Anglican Church Body in 1902, which was incorporated as a legal entity under his leadership as chairman.

In September 1906, Hoare led four theology students from St. Paul‚Äôs College (Ho Dao-pei, Cheng Yung-chi, Leung Kam-po, and Wong Ting-sang) on a missionary trip to Castle Peak by sea. On their return journey on September 18, they encountered the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon,‚Äù which capsized their boat. Both Hoare and the students perished, and their bodies were never recovered. Only Hoare‚Äôs prayer book was found, opened to Psalm 23:4:

‚ÄúEven though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for you are with me; your rod and your staff, they comfort me.‚Äù

On September 30, 1906, memorial services for Hoare were held simultaneously at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral in Hong Kong and Holy Trinity Cathedral in Shanghai. A commemorative stone was later placed in the Anglican Bishop‚Äôs House in Hong Kong in honor of Bishop Hoare.

(2) Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 21‚Äì22; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. b)

(2) ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ Gerald H. LanderÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö21-22; St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d bÔºâ

Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ, 1861‚Äì1934)

Gerald H. Lander served as the fifth Bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1907‚Äì1920). He graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained as a priest in 1885. In 1904, he became a pastor at Liverpool Cathedral, and in 1907, he was consecrated as bishop, succeeding Bishop Joseph C. Hoare, who had perished in the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon.‚Äù

The Church Missionary Society (CMS) sought to revive St. Paul‚Äôs College to provide Western-style education for Chinese students. They proposed relocating the existing seminary to China to attract more funding and students. In 1909, St. Paul‚Äôs College reopened under its original model, with the CMS taking full control of academic affairs. The bishop‚Äôs role became akin to that of a present-day school supervisor.

Beyond the college, Lander witnessed rapid growth in the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. In 1912, the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (the Chinese Anglican Church) was established as an independent province separate from the Church of England. In 1915, St. Paul‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College (now St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College) was founded. During the 1910s, both Diocesan Boys‚Äô School and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School acquired their current campuses, while St. Paul‚Äôs College itself expanded its facilities, doubling its enrollment capacity.

(3) Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ)

(Source: Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 108‚Äì112; Chung Yan Lap, 1975: 7‚Äì11; Zhong Hua Xu Xing Wei Ban Hui, 1921: 269)

(3) ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ Matthew A-JetÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2014aÔºö108-112ÔºõÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1975: 7-11Ôºõ‰∏≠ËèØÁ∫åË°åÂßîËæ¶ÊúÉ1921Ôºö269Ôºâ

Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ, 1840‚Äì1921)

Matthew A-Jet, a pastor, was born on September 3, 1840, in Shiliantang Village, Sanjia Market, Taishan County. In his early years, he worked at mines in Australia, where he became a Christian in Sydney in 1868 and later became a preacher. He returned to China in 1874 and joined Anglican missionaries on itinerant preaching tours in Zengcheng and Beihai. Upon arriving in Hong Kong, he expressed his calling to Bishop John S. Burdon of the Anglican Church and enrolled in the theological program at St. Paul‚Äôs College. At the time, the London Missionary Society (LMS) in Hong Kong lacked Chinese preachers. Upon A-Jet‚Äôs graduation, the LMS borrowed him from the Anglican Church to serve as a missionary. LMS missionaries E. J. Eitel and John Chalmers held A-Jet in high regard.

After serving as an LMS missionary for seven years, A-Jet joined the Anglican Church in 1883. On July 8, 1883, he was ordained as a deacon, becoming the second Chinese cleric ordained by the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. On March 9, 1884, he was ordained as a pastor and succeeded Luo Xin Yuan, who had retired, in leading the ministry of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. After moving to Kowloon in 1902, A-Jet became deeply involved in church work, actively promoting missionary and charitable efforts in Kowloon City and nearby areas. He made significant contributions to the establishment of the Holy Trinity Church parish.

While serving at Holy Trinity Church, A-Jet founded the Almshouse in 1902 to aid the poor, sick, and elderly. He later expanded it to include the Tian Guo Rescue Chapel. In 1903, he joined other Chinese pastors from the LMS and Baptist Church in petitioning the Hong Kong government to allocate land in Kowloon for a Chinese Christian cemetery. The government approved the request in 1904, creating the first Chinese Christian cemetery in Kowloon. In 1906, A-Jet established St. Paul‚Äôs Primary School (later renamed Tian Guo Primary School) in Kowloon Walled City to provide education for impoverished youth, teaching general subjects and vocational skills.

A-Jet also valued literary work and authored several books, including True Doctrine Clarified, Discerning Right from Wrong, and Paths of Heaven. He passed away on January 20, 1921, at the age of 81.

(4) ÁéãË¨ôÂ¶Ç Wong AnangÔºàÊùéÂøóÂâõ2014Ôºö133-136ÔºõÂºµÂøóÂÅâ2005Ôºö143-180Ôºâ

Wong Anang (1847‚Äì1907)

Wong Anang, born in Hong Kong in 1847, hailed from Dongguan, Guangdong. His father, Wong Yuan-shen, was the first preacher of the Rhenish Mission, and his elder brother was Wong Yuk-chu. As a young man, Wong graduated from the Rhenish Mission School and aspired to become a preacher. He studied theology under Ferdinand Genahr and Adam Jrolczyk. Wong graduated from the missionary school in 1866 and was ordained as a pastor in Yongfu in 1884. Over the years, he established churches in nearly ten locations, including:

  • 1877: Sun Wai Church

    • 1879: Fuyong, Dongguan, Xin‚Äôan

      • 1881: Shijia Village

        • 1886: Dongguan

          • 1895: Kaijingbei Church

            • 1896: Tangxia Yeshan

              • 1897: Taiping

                • 1898: Hong Kong

                  • 1902: Shiqi

                    • 1906: Daliang Dalang

                      Wong made significant contributions to the early missionary efforts of the Rhenish Mission.

                      In 1898, while recuperating in Hong Kong, Wong noticed that many believers from Dongguan and Bao‚Äôan had moved to Hong Kong, congregating in the Sai Ying Pun area. He used a missionary‚Äôs residence on Bonham Road West as a venue for Sunday sermons. After recovering, he returned to preach in Xiangshan, Shiqi, Daliang, and Xiaolan. In 1906, he settled in Hong Kong and participated in the planning and construction of the Rhenish Mission Church. Wong passed away in October 1907 at the age of

  1. The church building was completed in 1911, laying the foundation for the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs development in Hong Kong.

    Beyond preaching, Wong deeply reflected on the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture. He moved beyond the ‚ÄúAccommodation Theory‚Äù and ‚ÄúSupplementary Theory,‚Äù striving to demonstrate the superiority of Christianity in all aspects over Confucianism‚Äîa perspective he called the ‚ÄúTranscendence Theory.‚Äù He argued that Christianity was incomparable to Confucianism and firmly believed that Christian faith could lead to national prosperity and strength. Wong contributed articles to The Church News and A Review of the Times. In his later years, he authored works such as Christian and Confucian Monthly Commentary and The Measure of Three Religions.

    (5) Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú Anna M. DeaneÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö69-70ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö89-96Ôºâ

    Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú, ?‚Äì1918)

    Anna M. Deane arrived in Hong Kong in December 1909 as a member of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church from Virginia, USA. After her arrival, she assisted Alfred G. Garr at the ‚ÄúMissionary Home,‚Äù serving as the matron of the women‚Äôs dormitory. In April 1910, she collaborated with Mok Lai Chi to establish the ‚ÄúPentecostal English School,‚Äù where she served as the head teacher.

    In February 1911, Deane felt a calling to evangelize among fishermen. In September 1912, she set up a mission base for fishermen in Shau Kei Wan, founding the ‚ÄúAmerican Pentecostal Mission.‚Äù The mission included a chapel, a school, and a Sunday school. Additionally, she organized waterborne evangelism, regularly taking a small boat to preach the gospel to fishermen.

    In 1915, the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church merged with the Pentecostal Holiness Church, and the Shau Kei Wan chapel became the first Pentecostal Holiness Church in Hong Kong. Deane passed away in Hong Kong in 1918.

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 66‚Äì75; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì99)

    (6) Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Mok Lai ChiÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö66-75ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™2020Ôºö76-99Ôºâ

    Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫, 1867‚Äì1926)

    Mok Lai Chi, a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, was born into a Christian family. His father, Mok Wai Kam, was involved in promoting the self-reliance of To Tsai Church. Mok graduated from Queen‚Äôs College and worked as a civil servant, serving as a clerk at the Observatory, a police interpreter, and the chief translator for the Chinese Secretariat. He was originally a deacon and Sunday school principal at the American Congregational Church. Additionally, he founded Lok Kwan School, where he taught English to over 100 students.

    In 1907, Mok was hired as an interpreter for Alfred G. Garr. On November 10, 1907, he became the second individual to receive Spirit Baptism, making him a pioneer and founding figure of the Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong.

    The church was initially named the ‚ÄúApostolic Faith Church‚Äù but was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Pentecostal Mission‚Äù in 1910. In 1908, Mok launched the first Chinese Pentecostal publication, The Pentecostal Truth, introducing Pentecostal beliefs in Chinese for the first time. He also established the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission as a self-reliant Chinese church, playing a critical role in its growth and development.

    (7) Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 67‚Äì80; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì97)

    (7) ÂÆãÈºéÊñá Sung Teng ManÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö67-80ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö76-97Ôºâ

    Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá, 1883‚Äì1958)

    Sung Teng Man, a second-generation Christian, was baptized during his childhood in Guangzhou and later joined the American Congregational Church in Hong Kong. In 1901, after the completion of the new church building, disputes arose between Chinese members and the missionary society over property rights. Dissatisfied with how the issue was handled, Sung left the church.

    In 1907, when the Pentecostal movement reached Hong Kong, Pastor A. G. Garr and his wife arrived and borrowed the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street for meetings. They emphasized teachings on repentance, forgiveness, and the baptism of the Holy Spirit. Sung, who accompanied his wife to these gatherings, experienced a renewed infilling of the Holy Spirit and became the third believer to receive Spirit Baptism (on November 15, 1907). However, opponents accused the group of heresy and prohibited others from attending their services. In response, Garr, Mok Lai Chi, and Sung Teng Man co-founded the Pentecostal Mission (initially named the Apostolic Faith Church).

    After Mok Lai Chi passed away in 1926, Sung became the leader of the church. In 1939, following his retirement from a senior civil service position, he was ordained as a pastor. Sung guided the church through the three years and eight months of Japanese occupation during World War

  1. He passed away in 1958.

    Following his death, the Kowloon branch of the Pentecostal Mission experienced a split, leading some members to establish the ‚ÄúKowloon Pentecostal Church.‚Äù A legal battle over property rights ensued.

    (8) ÈúçÈùúÂ±± Fok Ching ShanÔºàÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤1996Ôºö327-329ÔºõÈô≥ÁùøÊñá2023Ôºö47-59ÔºõÊííÊØçËÄ≥1919Ôºö 17-18Ôºâ

    Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±, 1851‚Äì1918)

    Fok Ching Shan, born in 1851 in Shunde, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong as a child and later decided to pursue theological studies. He enrolled in St. Paul‚Äôs Theological School under the Anglican Church in Hong Kong, where he remained as a teacher after graduation. Alongside teaching, he assisted with missionary work at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church.

    In 1902, when Pastor Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ) relocated to Kowloon to oversee the ministry at Holy Trinity Church, the need for a parish rector became urgent. At that time, Pastor A-Jet was managing both St. Stephen‚Äôs Church and Holy Trinity Church. In 1903, Fok was ordained as a deacon and became a pastor the following year, succeeding Matthew A-Jet as the rector of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. During his tenure, Fok established a parish council to promote church autonomy and encouraged congregants to contribute financially to achieve self-sufficiency.

    Fok also established a women‚Äôs Bible school within the church to train female evangelists. Beyond St. Stephen‚Äôs Church, he managed All Saints‚Äô Church in Yau Ma Tei and the newly established St. Paul‚Äôs Church. He also founded the Joint Parish Council, where he served as vice-chairman. Fok dedicated 50 years of his life to church service.

    Fok Ching Shan passed away in March 1918 at the age of 67. His passing was deeply mourned by the church community, with thousands of clergy and believers attending his funeral. On March 9, 1919, Bishop Gerald H. Lander of the Diocese of Hong Kong and South China presided over the dedication of a memorial plaque for Fok Ching Shan, recognizing his significant contributions to the church.

    Ôºà9ÔºâÁøÅÊå∫Áîü Yung ParkÔºàÂäâÁëûÊªî1975Ôºö25-32 Ôºâ

    Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü, 1865‚Äì1955)

    Born in Taishan, Guangdong, Yung Park traveled to Hong Kong at the age of 17 before working in the gold mines of San Francisco for four years. He later transitioned to working as a laborer. During this time, he encountered Christianity while studying English at a Gospel Hall and was baptized at the Bethany Chapel in San Francisco in December 1891. The following year, he moved to New York for work and began attending the Congregational Church‚Äôs True Seeking Hall.

    After five years, feeling the need to bring the Gospel to his hometown, Yung raised over 800 gold dollars from fellow believers and returned to Taishan, where he established a Gospel Hall near a temple. In 1903, he moved to Hong Kong and was appointed to the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street. He was ordained as a pastor in 1904.

    In 1912, the American Congregational Church was renamed the ‚ÄúChinese Congregational Church.‚Äù Under Yung‚Äôs leadership, the church established branches in Foshan, Mong Kok, Taishan, Zhongshan, Causeway Bay, and Shau Kei Wan. In 1918, he was elected as the chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council, becoming the first Chinese to hold this position since the council‚Äôs founding in 1915.

    Yung retired in 1947 due to a brain illness and passed away in February 1955 at the age of 91.

(1) Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü)

(Source: Chung Yan Lap, 1951: 17‚Äì20; St. Paul‚Äôs College, n.d. a)

(1) ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü Joseph C. HoareÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö17-20Ôºõ St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d aÔºâ

Joseph C. Hoare (ÈúçÁ¥ÑÁëü, 1851‚Äì1906)

Joseph C. Hoare was the fourth bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1898‚Äì1906). Born in 1851, he graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained into the clergy. Sent by the Church Missionary Society (CMS) to China, Hoare established Trinity Theological School in Ningbo, Zhejiang, where he served as principal. During his 20 years in Ningbo, he became proficient in the Ningbo dialect and developed a unique educational model, ‚Äúevangelical theory through ambulando‚Äù (‚Äúlearning theology through missionary practice‚Äù). This method emphasized hands-on training, with Hoare personally leading students on preaching journeys to remote villages. These missions often lasted up to ten weeks, blending demonstration and instruction. Under his leadership, Trinity Theological School trained dozens of Chinese missionaries, becoming a premier institution in the region.

On June 11, 1898, Hoare was consecrated as the Bishop of Victoria at St. Paul‚Äôs Cathedral in London and was posted to Hong Kong.

In 1901, Hoare reorganized St. Paul‚Äôs College into a theological seminary focused on training Chinese clergy. He reached an agreement with the CMS, whereby the bishop directly managed the teaching while the CMS provided additional staff and resources. During his tenure, the Chinese Anglican community in Hong Kong expanded, adding Holy Trinity Church and All Saints‚Äô Church alongside St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. Hoare also established the Chinese Anglican Church Body in 1902, which was incorporated as a legal entity under his leadership as chairman.

In September 1906, Hoare led four theology students from St. Paul‚Äôs College (Ho Dao-pei, Cheng Yung-chi, Leung Kam-po, and Wong Ting-sang) on a missionary trip to Castle Peak by sea. On their return journey on September 18, they encountered the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon,‚Äù which capsized their boat. Both Hoare and the students perished, and their bodies were never recovered. Only Hoare‚Äôs prayer book was found, opened to Psalm 23:4:

‚ÄúEven though I walk through the valley of the shadow of death, I will fear no evil, for you are with me; your rod and your staff, they comfort me.‚Äù

On September 30, 1906, memorial services for Hoare were held simultaneously at St. John‚Äôs Cathedral in Hong Kong and Holy Trinity Cathedral in Shanghai. A commemorative stone was later placed in the Anglican Bishop‚Äôs House in Hong Kong in honor of Bishop Hoare.

(2) Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ)

(2) ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ Gerald H. LanderÔºàÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1951Ôºö21-22; St. Paul‚Äôs College n.d bÔºâ

Gerald H. Lander (ÂÄ´Áæ©ËèØ, 1861‚Äì1934)

Gerald H. Lander served as the fifth Bishop of the Anglican Diocese of Victoria (1907‚Äì1920). He graduated from Trinity College, Cambridge, and was ordained as a priest in 1885. In 1904, he became a pastor at Liverpool Cathedral, and in 1907, he was consecrated as bishop, succeeding Bishop Joseph C. Hoare, who had perished in the ‚ÄúBingwu Typhoon.‚Äù

The Church Missionary Society (CMS) sought to revive St. Paul‚Äôs College to provide Western-style education for Chinese students. They proposed relocating the existing seminary to China to attract more funding and students. In 1909, St. Paul‚Äôs College reopened under its original model, with the CMS taking full control of academic affairs. The bishop‚Äôs role became akin to that of a present-day school supervisor.

Beyond the college, Lander witnessed rapid growth in the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. In 1912, the Chung Hua Sheng Kung Hui (the Chinese Anglican Church) was established as an independent province separate from the Church of England. In 1915, St. Paul‚Äôs Girls‚Äô College (now St. Paul‚Äôs Co-educational College) was founded. During the 1910s, both Diocesan Boys‚Äô School and Diocesan Girls‚Äô School acquired their current campuses, while St. Paul‚Äôs College itself expanded its facilities, doubling its enrollment capacity.

(3) Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ)

(Source: Xing Fuzeng, 2014a: 108‚Äì112; Chung Yan Lap, 1975: 7‚Äì11; Zhong Hua Xu Xing Wei Ban Hui, 1921: 269)

(3) ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ Matthew A-JetÔºàÈÇ¢Á¶èÂ¢û2014aÔºö108-112ÔºõÈçæ‰ªÅÁ´ã1975: 7-11Ôºõ‰∏≠ËèØÁ∫åË°åÂßîËæ¶ÊúÉ1921Ôºö269Ôºâ

Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ, 1840‚Äì1921)

Matthew A-Jet, a pastor, was born on September 3, 1840, in Shiliantang Village, Sanjia Market, Taishan County. In his early years, he worked at mines in Australia, where he became a Christian in Sydney in 1868 and later became a preacher. He returned to China in 1874 and joined Anglican missionaries on itinerant preaching tours in Zengcheng and Beihai. Upon arriving in Hong Kong, he expressed his calling to Bishop John S. Burdon of the Anglican Church and enrolled in the theological program at St. Paul‚Äôs College. At the time, the London Missionary Society (LMS) in Hong Kong lacked Chinese preachers. Upon A-Jet‚Äôs graduation, the LMS borrowed him from the Anglican Church to serve as a missionary. LMS missionaries E. J. Eitel and John Chalmers held A-Jet in high regard.

After serving as an LMS missionary for seven years, A-Jet joined the Anglican Church in 1883. On July 8, 1883, he was ordained as a deacon, becoming the second Chinese cleric ordained by the Anglican Church in Hong Kong. On March 9, 1884, he was ordained as a pastor and succeeded Luo Xin Yuan, who had retired, in leading the ministry of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. After moving to Kowloon in 1902, A-Jet became deeply involved in church work, actively promoting missionary and charitable efforts in Kowloon City and nearby areas. He made significant contributions to the establishment of the Holy Trinity Church parish.

While serving at Holy Trinity Church, A-Jet founded the Almshouse in 1902 to aid the poor, sick, and elderly. He later expanded it to include the Tian Guo Rescue Chapel. In 1903, he joined other Chinese pastors from the LMS and Baptist Church in petitioning the Hong Kong government to allocate land in Kowloon for a Chinese Christian cemetery. The government approved the request in 1904, creating the first Chinese Christian cemetery in Kowloon. In 1906, A-Jet established St. Paul‚Äôs Primary School (later renamed Tian Guo Primary School) in Kowloon Walled City to provide education for impoverished youth, teaching general subjects and vocational skills.

A-Jet also valued literary work and authored several books, including True Doctrine Clarified, Discerning Right from Wrong, and Paths of Heaven. He passed away on January 20, 1921, at the age of 81.

(4) ÁéãË¨ôÂ¶Ç Wong AnangÔºàÊùéÂøóÂâõ2014Ôºö133-136ÔºõÂºµÂøóÂÅâ2005Ôºö143-180Ôºâ

Wong Anang (1847‚Äì1907)

Wong Anang, born in Hong Kong in 1847, hailed from Dongguan, Guangdong. His father, Wong Yuan-shen, was the first preacher of the Rhenish Mission, and his elder brother was Wong Yuk-chu. As a young man, Wong graduated from the Rhenish Mission School and aspired to become a preacher. He studied theology under Ferdinand Genahr and Adam Jrolczyk. Wong graduated from the missionary school in 1866 and was ordained as a pastor in Yongfu in 1884. Over the years, he established churches in nearly ten locations, including:

  • 1877: Sun Wai Church

    • 1879: Fuyong, Dongguan, Xin‚Äôan

      • 1881: Shijia Village

        • 1886: Dongguan

          • 1895: Kaijingbei Church

            • 1896: Tangxia Yeshan

              • 1897: Taiping

                • 1898: Hong Kong

                  • 1902: Shiqi

                    • 1906: Daliang Dalang

                      Wong made significant contributions to the early missionary efforts of the Rhenish Mission.

                      In 1898, while recuperating in Hong Kong, Wong noticed that many believers from Dongguan and Bao‚Äôan had moved to Hong Kong, congregating in the Sai Ying Pun area. He used a missionary‚Äôs residence on Bonham Road West as a venue for Sunday sermons. After recovering, he returned to preach in Xiangshan, Shiqi, Daliang, and Xiaolan. In 1906, he settled in Hong Kong and participated in the planning and construction of the Rhenish Mission Church. Wong passed away in October 1907 at the age of

  1. The church building was completed in 1911, laying the foundation for the Rhenish Mission‚Äôs development in Hong Kong.

    Beyond preaching, Wong deeply reflected on the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture. He moved beyond the ‚ÄúAccommodation Theory‚Äù and ‚ÄúSupplementary Theory,‚Äù striving to demonstrate the superiority of Christianity in all aspects over Confucianism‚Äîa perspective he called the ‚ÄúTranscendence Theory.‚Äù He argued that Christianity was incomparable to Confucianism and firmly believed that Christian faith could lead to national prosperity and strength. Wong contributed articles to The Church News and A Review of the Times. In his later years, he authored works such as Christian and Confucian Monthly Commentary and The Measure of Three Religions.

    (5) Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú Anna M. DeaneÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö69-70ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö89-96Ôºâ

    Anna M. Deane (Áî∞ÂÆâÂ®ú, ?‚Äì1918)

    Anna M. Deane arrived in Hong Kong in December 1909 as a member of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church from Virginia, USA. After her arrival, she assisted Alfred G. Garr at the ‚ÄúMissionary Home,‚Äù serving as the matron of the women‚Äôs dormitory. In April 1910, she collaborated with Mok Lai Chi to establish the ‚ÄúPentecostal English School,‚Äù where she served as the head teacher.

    In February 1911, Deane felt a calling to evangelize among fishermen. In September 1912, she set up a mission base for fishermen in Shau Kei Wan, founding the ‚ÄúAmerican Pentecostal Mission.‚Äù The mission included a chapel, a school, and a Sunday school. Additionally, she organized waterborne evangelism, regularly taking a small boat to preach the gospel to fishermen.

    In 1915, the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church merged with the Pentecostal Holiness Church, and the Shau Kei Wan chapel became the first Pentecostal Holiness Church in Hong Kong. Deane passed away in Hong Kong in 1918.
