    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 66‚Äì75; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì99)

    (6) Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫Mok Lai ChiÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö66-75ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™2020Ôºö76-99Ôºâ

    Mok Lai Chi (Ëé´Á¶ÆÊô∫, 1867‚Äì1926)

    Mok Lai Chi, a native of Xin‚Äôan, Guangdong, was born into a Christian family. His father, Mok Wai Kam, was involved in promoting the self-reliance of To Tsai Church. Mok graduated from Queen‚Äôs College and worked as a civil servant, serving as a clerk at the Observatory, a police interpreter, and the chief translator for the Chinese Secretariat. He was originally a deacon and Sunday school principal at the American Congregational Church. Additionally, he founded Lok Kwan School, where he taught English to over 100 students.

    In 1907, Mok was hired as an interpreter for Alfred G. Garr. On November 10, 1907, he became the second individual to receive Spirit Baptism, making him a pioneer and founding figure of the Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong.

    The church was initially named the ‚ÄúApostolic Faith Church‚Äù but was renamed the ‚ÄúHong Kong Pentecostal Mission‚Äù in 1910. In 1908, Mok launched the first Chinese Pentecostal publication, The Pentecostal Truth, introducing Pentecostal beliefs in Chinese for the first time. He also established the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission as a self-reliant Chinese church, playing a critical role in its growth and development.

    (7) Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá)

    (Source: Ye Xianqin, 2021: 67‚Äì80; Dai Guanhao, 2020: 76‚Äì97)

    (7) ÂÆãÈºéÊñá Sung Teng ManÔºàËëâÂÖàÁß¶2021Ôºö67-80ÔºõÊà¥ËßÄË±™ 2020Ôºö76-97Ôºâ

    Sung Teng Man (ÂÆãÈºéÊñá, 1883‚Äì1958)

    Sung Teng Man, a second-generation Christian, was baptized during his childhood in Guangzhou and later joined the American Congregational Church in Hong Kong. In 1901, after the completion of the new church building, disputes arose between Chinese members and the missionary society over property rights. Dissatisfied with how the issue was handled, Sung left the church.

    In 1907, when the Pentecostal movement reached Hong Kong, Pastor A. G. Garr and his wife arrived and borrowed the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street for meetings. They emphasized teachings on repentance, forgiveness, and the baptism of the Holy Spirit. Sung, who accompanied his wife to these gatherings, experienced a renewed infilling of the Holy Spirit and became the third believer to receive Spirit Baptism (on November 15, 1907). However, opponents accused the group of heresy and prohibited others from attending their services. In response, Garr, Mok Lai Chi, and Sung Teng Man co-founded the Pentecostal Mission (initially named the Apostolic Faith Church).

    After Mok Lai Chi passed away in 1926, Sung became the leader of the church. In 1939, following his retirement from a senior civil service position, he was ordained as a pastor. Sung guided the church through the three years and eight months of Japanese occupation during World War

  1. He passed away in 1958.

    Following his death, the Kowloon branch of the Pentecostal Mission experienced a split, leading some members to establish the ‚ÄúKowloon Pentecostal Church.‚Äù A legal battle over property rights ensued.

    (8) ÈúçÈùúÂ±± Fok Ching ShanÔºàÂäâÁ≤µËÅ≤1996Ôºö327-329ÔºõÈô≥ÁùøÊñá2023Ôºö47-59ÔºõÊííÊØçËÄ≥1919Ôºö 17-18Ôºâ

    Fok Ching Shan (ÈúçÈùúÂ±±, 1851‚Äì1918)

    Fok Ching Shan, born in 1851 in Shunde, Guangdong, moved to Hong Kong as a child and later decided to pursue theological studies. He enrolled in St. Paul‚Äôs Theological School under the Anglican Church in Hong Kong, where he remained as a teacher after graduation. Alongside teaching, he assisted with missionary work at St. Stephen‚Äôs Church.

    In 1902, when Pastor Matthew A-Jet (ÈÑ∫Êó•‰øÆ) relocated to Kowloon to oversee the ministry at Holy Trinity Church, the need for a parish rector became urgent. At that time, Pastor A-Jet was managing both St. Stephen‚Äôs Church and Holy Trinity Church. In 1903, Fok was ordained as a deacon and became a pastor the following year, succeeding Matthew A-Jet as the rector of St. Stephen‚Äôs Church. During his tenure, Fok established a parish council to promote church autonomy and encouraged congregants to contribute financially to achieve self-sufficiency.

    Fok also established a women‚Äôs Bible school within the church to train female evangelists. Beyond St. Stephen‚Äôs Church, he managed All Saints‚Äô Church in Yau Ma Tei and the newly established St. Paul‚Äôs Church. He also founded the Joint Parish Council, where he served as vice-chairman. Fok dedicated 50 years of his life to church service.

    Fok Ching Shan passed away in March 1918 at the age of 67. His passing was deeply mourned by the church community, with thousands of clergy and believers attending his funeral. On March 9, 1919, Bishop Gerald H. Lander of the Diocese of Hong Kong and South China presided over the dedication of a memorial plaque for Fok Ching Shan, recognizing his significant contributions to the church.

    Ôºà9ÔºâÁøÅÊå∫Áîü Yung ParkÔºàÂäâÁëûÊªî1975Ôºö25-32 Ôºâ

    Yung Park (ÁøÅÊå∫Áîü, 1865‚Äì1955)

    Born in Taishan, Guangdong, Yung Park traveled to Hong Kong at the age of 17 before working in the gold mines of San Francisco for four years. He later transitioned to working as a laborer. During this time, he encountered Christianity while studying English at a Gospel Hall and was baptized at the Bethany Chapel in San Francisco in December 1891. The following year, he moved to New York for work and began attending the Congregational Church‚Äôs True Seeking Hall.

    After five years, feeling the need to bring the Gospel to his hometown, Yung raised over 800 gold dollars from fellow believers and returned to Taishan, where he established a Gospel Hall near a temple. In 1903, he moved to Hong Kong and was appointed to the American Congregational Church on Ladder Street. He was ordained as a pastor in 1904.

    In 1912, the American Congregational Church was renamed the ‚ÄúChinese Congregational Church.‚Äù Under Yung‚Äôs leadership, the church established branches in Foshan, Mong Kok, Taishan, Zhongshan, Causeway Bay, and Shau Kei Wan. In 1918, he was elected as the chairman of the Hong Kong Christian Council, becoming the first Chinese to hold this position since the council‚Äôs founding in 1915.

    Yung retired in 1947 due to a brain illness and passed away in February 1955 at the age of 91.

    Christian Population in Hong Kong (Xing Fuzeng 2004:4; Hong Kong Government 1900, 1909a, 1909b)

    According to the ‚ÄúEcclesiastical Return‚Äù statistics included in the Hong Kong Government‚Äôs Blue Book, between 1900 and 1909, the attendance at Chinese churches began to surpass that of non-Chinese churches and continued to grow steadily. In 1901, the attendance at Chinese churches was 1,205, which increased to 2,080 by 1909‚Äîa growth rate of 72.6%. In comparison, the attendance at non-Chinese churches during the same period grew from 1,230 to 1,580, an increase of 28.4%. As for baptisms, Chinese churches recorded significantly more than Western churches in 1909, with 266 baptisms compared to 74.

    However, the Chinese churches recorded in the Blue Book were limited to mainstream denominations such as the Anglican Church, London Missionary Society, Congregational Church, Methodist Church, Basel Mission, and Rhenish Mission.

    In 1909, Hong Kong‚Äôs total population was 319,803, of which 12,415 were non-Chinese, and 307,388 were Chinese. This means that the proportion of Chinese Christians (worship attendees) among the Chinese population was approximately 0.67%, slightly higher than the 0.4% recorded in 1899.

    1909Âπ¥È¶ôÊ∏Ø‰∫∫Âè£Á∏Ω‰∫∫Âè£ÁÇ∫319,803ÔºåÂÖ∂‰∏≠ÈùûËèØ‰∫∫Êï∏ÁõÆÁÇ∫12,415ÔºåËèØ‰∫∫ÁÇ∫307,388„ÄÇÊèõË®Ä‰πãÔºåËèØ‰∫∫Âü∫Áù£Âæí(Âá∫Â∏≠Â¥áÊãúËÄÖ)‰ΩîÈ¶ôÊ∏ØËèØ‰∫∫‰∫∫Âè£ÁöÑÊØî‰æãÁ¥ÑÁÇ∫0.67%ÔºåÁï•È´òÊñº1899Âπ¥ÁöÑ0.4%„ÄÇ

    (1) Sailors and Soldiers Home, 1901 (Wen Kuo-Wei 1992: 10-17)

    The British Methodist Church prioritized China in its missionary work and had no initial plans to establish a base in Hong Kong. Even when resources were allocated to Hong Kong, the focus was on serving Westerners, particularly Methodist members stationed with the military. In 1893, the Soldiers‚Äô Church was completed. In 1896, Reverend W. Musson of the Soldiers‚Äô Church, concerned about the rising number of bars in Hong Kong, proposed creating a wholesome recreation center for soldiers. He wrote to the War Department, applying to establish the Sailors and Soldiers Home. In 1897, the Hong Kong government allocated land on Arsenal Street for construction. The facility officially opened in 1901, featuring 100 beds and amenities such as a coffee lounge, reading rooms, billiard tables, a prayer room, and a meeting hall.

    In 1924, due to reclamation work, the government reclaimed the Arsenal Street site and allocated a new plot on Hennessy Road for rebuilding the Home. The new facility was completed in 1929 and inaugurated by Governor Sir Cecil Clementi. The work paused during the Japanese occupation of Hong Kong but resumed after the war, with the Home directly managed by the British mission. By the 1970s, with the decline in the number of British military personnel in Hong Kong, the mission agreed to repurpose the facility. In 1975, the Methodist Church and the United Church merged, and the original property was handed over to the United Church. In 1980, the Methodist-United Christian Center was established at the site, focusing on youth services, later expanding to children and adolescent services. In 1987, the Methodist-United Church decided to rebuild the facility. Partnering with a real estate developer, a new 11-story building (Wai Lam Court) was completed in 1991, providing 250 guest rooms. The Methodist-United Christian Center relocated to Wan Chai to continue serving the community.

    (2) Hong Kong Baptist Church, 1901 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 63-67; Lee Kam-Keung 2002: 37-59; Xu Songshi 1971: 12-16; Wong 1974)

    After J. L. Shuck and Issacher Roberts left Hong Kong in 1844, the Southern Baptist Convention shifted its missionary focus to Guangzhou. In 1881, Mrs. Johnson from the Northern Baptist Convention, due to her advanced age, handed over her church to the Southern Baptist Convention. Under the leadership of Reverend Simmons, the congregation transitioned from Chaozhou dialect to Cantonese. In 1886, Reverend Fung Wat-Chuen, returning from the U.S., was appointed as pastor. With an increasing number of Chinese American immigrants residing in Hong Kong, the community funded the construction of a new church, relocating several times during the process.

    In 1896, Mrs. Vanderpool, a devoted member, observed the inadequacy of the meeting space and advocated for purchasing a property for worship. In 1899, the church acquired a site at 51 Peel Street, and in 1901, the new church, named the Hong Kong Baptist Church, was completed. Mrs. Vanderpool wrote to the U.S., inviting Reverend Tang Kit-King to serve in Hong Kong. By 1911, the church had over 300 members. In 1912, the church acquired a property at 50 Hollywood Road, later relocating to Caine Road in 1920. By the end of 1922, the new church was completed. The Hong Kong Baptist Church actively engaged in missionary work, establishing congregations in areas like Yau Ma Tei, Zhongshan Shiqi, Aberdeen, Hung Hom, Shau Kei Wan, and Kowloon City, which later became independent churches.

    (3) Chinese YMCA, 1901 (Ding Sin-Pao 2008: 166-180; Hong Kong Chinese YMCA, n.d.)

    During the 1890s, Young Men‚Äôs Christian Associations (YMCAs) were successively established at institutions like Queen‚Äôs College, St. Paul‚Äôs College, Diocesan Boys‚Äô School, and the Hong Kong College of Medicine. In 1898, F. S. Brockman, an official from the North American YMCA, delivered speeches in Hong Kong advocating for the formation of an urban YMCA. At the time, Yin Wen-Kai, employed at the Hong Kong College of Medicine, wrote to the British and American YMCAs requesting assistance in managing the association. The British declined due to personnel and financial constraints, but the Americans sent W. J. Southam to Hong Kong.

    In 1901, a premises at 27 Des Voeux Road Central was rented as the YMCA‚Äôs headquarters, consolidating associations from various schools. Between 1901 and 1907, it operated as the Hong Kong YMCA, divided into Chinese and Western sections. By 1908, the two sections became fully independent, each with its own board. The Chinese branch was renamed the Hong Kong Chinese YMCA in 1911.

    In 1918, the central clubhouse on Bridges Street was completed, incorporating traditional Chinese architectural elements like red bricks and green tiles, alongside modern facilities such as Hong Kong‚Äôs first indoor swimming pool and a cinder running track. The venue also hosted notable events, such as Lu Xun‚Äôs 1927 lecture. After 1966, the headquarters relocated to Kowloon.

    (4) Seventh-Day Adventist Mission, 1902 (Yang Jian-Sheng 2002: 32-35, 252; 706-707, 770-772)

    The Seventh-Day Adventist Mission in Hong Kong traces its origins to Abram La Rue (1822‚Äì1903), the movement‚Äôs first pioneer. Born in New Jersey, USA, La Rue became an Adventist believer, harboring a lifelong ambition for overseas missionary work. In 1883, he established the first Seventh-Day Adventist Church in Honolulu but continued to pursue his dream of preaching in China. Arriving in Hong Kong in 1888 at age 66, he distributed literature to English-speaking residents, sailors, and Chinese individuals proficient in English. From Hong Kong, he expanded his mission to Guangzhou, Shanghai, Japan, Singapore, and Java.

    In 1901, Jacob N. Anderson and his wife, accompanied by Ida Thompson, arrived in Hong Kong under the General Conference‚Äôs direction. In 1902, they joined La Rue. By 1903, Anderson baptized six Chinese individuals. La Rue passed away in April 1903. By 1941, the Adventist Church had established five congregations in Hong Kong.

    Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÂæ©Ëá®ÂÆâÊÅØÊó•ÊúÉÔºàSeventh-Day Adventist MissionÔºâÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁöÑÂª∫Á´ãÔºåÂèØËøΩÊ∫ØËá≥Ë©≤ÊúÉÈ¶ñ‰ΩçÂÖàÈãíÊãâË∑Ø‰∫û‰ºØËò≠ÔºàAbram La Rue, 1822‚Äì1903Ôºâ„ÄÇÊãâË∑ØÊñº1822Âπ¥11Êúà25Êó•ÁîüÊñºÁæéÂúãÊñ∞Êæ§Ë•øÂ∑ûÔºåÂæåÊàêÁÇ∫Âæ©Ëá®ÈÅãÂãï‰ø°Âæí„ÄÇÂ∞ëÂπ¥Â∑≤Á´ãÂøóÂà∞ÂúãÂ§ñÂÆ£ÊïôÔºåÊõæÂú®„ÄåÂìàÈ≠ØËå≤Â†°Â≠∏Èô¢„ÄçÊé•ÂèóÁü≠ÊúüË£ùÂÇôÔºå‰∏¶ÂêëÂÖ®ÁêÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÁî≥Ë´ãÂà∞‰∏≠ÂúãÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå‰ΩÜÁ∏ΩÊúÉÂª∫Ë≠∞‰ªñÂà∞Â§™Âπ≥Ê¥ãÂ≥∂Â∂º‰ΩàÈÅì„ÄÇ1883Âπ¥ÔºåÂú®Ê™ÄÈ¶ôÂ±±Âª∫Á´ãÈ¶ñÈñìÂæ©Ëá®ÂÆâÊÅØÊó•ÊúÉ„ÄÇ‰ΩÜ‰ªñ‰ªçÊ≤íÊúâÂøòË®òÂà∞‰∏≠ÂúãÂÇ≥ÊïôÁöÑÂøÉÂøóÔºå‰∏¶Êñº1888Âπ¥5Êúà3Êó•Ôºå‰ª•66Ê≠≤È´òÈΩ°ÊäµÊ∏Ø„ÄÇ‰ªñÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÊ¥æÁôºÊõ∏ÂàäÔºå‰∏ªË¶ÅÂêëÂØìÊ∏ØËã±‰∫∫ÂèäÂ§ñÁ±çÊ∞¥ÊâãÔºåÂèäÊáÇËã±Ë™ûÁöÑËèØ‰∫∫ÂÇ≥Êïô„ÄÇ‰ªñ‰ª•È¶ôÊ∏ØÁÇ∫Ê†πÊìöÂú∞ÔºåÂåó‰∏äÂª£Â∑û„ÄÅ‰∏äÊµ∑Ôºå‰ª•ÂèäÊó•Êú¨„ÄÅÊñ∞Âä†Âù°„ÄÅÂ©ÜÁæÖÂ∑û„ÄÅÁà™Âìá‰∏ÄÂ∏∂ÂÇ≥Êïô„ÄÇÁÇ∫‰∫ÜÂêëËèØ‰∫∫ÂÆ£ÊïôÔºå‰ªñÂú®‰∏Ä‰ΩçËèØ‰∫∫ÂçîÂä©‰∏ãÔºåÂ∞á„ÄäÂØ©Âà§„ÄãË≠ØÊàê‰∏≠ÊñáÔºåÊñº1891Âπ¥Âá∫ÁâàÔºåÂæå‰æÜÂèàÁøªË≠ØÊá∑Â∏´ÊØçÁöÑ„ÄäÊãæÁ¥öÂ∞±‰∏ª„Äã„ÄÇ

    1901Âπ¥4ÊúàÔºåÂÖ®ÁêÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÊ≠£ÂºèÊ¥æÂÆâÂæ∑Á¥îÔºàJacob N. AndersonÔºâÂ§´Â©¶Âà∞È¶ôÊ∏ØÔºåÊñºÊú¨Ë®àÂäÉÂà∞Â∑¥Ë•øÁöÑË≠öÊÑõÂæ∑ÔºàIda ThompsonÔºåÂÆâÂ∏´ÊØç‰πãÂ¶πÂ¶πÔºâ‰πüÈö®ÂÖ©‰∫∫ÂêåË°å„ÄÇ‰∏â‰∫∫Êñº1902Âπ¥2ÊúàÊäµÊ∏ØÔºåÊãâË∑ØÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØËøéÊé•„ÄÇ1903Âπ¥2ÊúàÔºåÂÆâÂæ∑Á¥îÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÁÇ∫6ÂêçËèØ‰∫∫ËàâË°åÊµ∏Á¶Æ„ÄÇ1903Âπ¥4Êúà26Êó•ÔºåÊãâË∑ØÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÈÄù‰∏ñ„ÄÇËá≥1941Âπ¥ÔºåÂÆâÊÅØÊó•ÊúÉÂú®È¶ôÊ∏ØÂÖàÂæåÂª∫‰∫Ü‰∫Ü5ÊâÄÊïôÊúÉ„ÄÇ

    (5) Chinese Christian Church, 1903 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 74-77; Mak Mui-Sang 1924: 182-188)

    At the beginning of the 20th century, the Chinese Christian Self-Independence Movement emerged. In 1902, the Chinese Christian Association was founded in Shanghai, advocating for church independence. Huang Hung-Kee, a believer returning from the U.S., brought the association‚Äôs constitution to Hong Kong and discussed establishing a church with Ng Hon-Chi. In 1903, a group of about ten believers rented a property on Shanghai Street, Yau Ma Tei, to establish a church, electing Ng as the chairperson. At the time, the church had no fixed premises.

    In 1905, Reverend Deng Cho, returning from Australia, served as the church‚Äôs volunteer pastor and later as its chairman. In 1906, he promoted the church‚Äôs independence in Haifeng, establishing a branch there, followed by another in Guangzhou the next year. In 1910, Reverend Wong Kai-Sun succeeded Deng. By 1911, the church set up a chapel in Sai Wan Ho. In 1926, the church joined the Hong Kong Christian Council as a member. In 1931, the church purchased and inaugurated a property on Dundas Street, Kowloon.

    (6) Hong Kong Christian Mutual Improvement Society, 1903 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 276-278; Yeung Kwok-Keung 2010: 63-64)

    The Hong Kong Christian Mutual Improvement Society was initiated by Christian leaders Ho Hin-Wing, Ho Lok-Kam, and Deng Hip-Chi. Its purpose was mutual care during hardship and illness, including visitation, support, and the promotion of moral and intellectual development. Initially, it operated from the Wesleyan Gospel Chapel and later rented premises at the Union Chapel. In 1913, the society purchased a building on Peel Street, Central.

    The society‚Äôs activities were divided into eight departments: moral education, intellectual development, community affairs, literary work, charity, finance, visitation, and general affairs. By 1925, membership exceeded 3,000. The society also focused on spreading Christian teachings, with an average of 15 members converting each year. It organized free medical services for impoverished members and provided funeral assistance. Additionally, it supported disaster relief in war-torn and famine-stricken regions in China.

    The society published a weekly journal, initially titled Heavenly Heart News, later restructured as Weekly Magazine in 1925, featuring articles, church news, and summaries of Sunday sermons.

    (7) Tsung Tsin Mission, 1903 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 162-163; Nicole Constable 1994)

    The Basel Mission, which focused on the Hakka community, established churches primarily in Hakka-majority areas. The Tsung Tsin Mission, located in Lung Yeuk Tau, Fanling, was founded in 1903 by retired pastor Ling Kai-Lin. Ling had previously evangelized in Guangdong and purchased land in Lung Yeuk Tau upon retirement.

    Although Lung Yeuk Tau was not a Hakka-majority area and was dominated by the Tang clan, Hakka tenant farmers gradually settled there. Ling evangelized to these farmers and other Hakka residents, resulting in 10 converts by 1905. The Basel Mission later assigned Reverend Peng Lok-Sam to formally establish the church. Over time, a village formed around the church, named Tsung Tsin Village.

    In 1927, a new church was built to accommodate the growing congregation. Infrastructure like roads, bridges, and a primary school were developed alongside the church. By 1931, a cemetery was approved for the church by the New Territories Administration. The Tsung Tsin Mission became a model Christian village, combining Hakka culture with Christianity.

    (8) New Territories Evangelization Society, 1904 (Lo Pui-Yee 2009; Wong Chi-Sun 1986: 43-44; Lau Shao-Lin 2003: 140-144)

    In 1898, Britain leased the New Territories, integrating it into Hong Kong as a colony. This expansion enabled Christian missions to begin work in the region. The New Territories Evangelization Society (NTES), established in 1904, was a collaboration between the Union Chapel and To Tsai Church of the London Missionary Society.

    The NTES quickly built churches and schools in several areas, including Yuen Long (1898), Tsuen Wan (1904), Cheung Chau (1904), Tai Po, Lam Tsuen (1906), Tai O (1906), Sheung Shui (1909), San Tin (1913), and Tuen Mun (1915). By 1932, the NTES was incorporated into the Sixth District Association of the Guangdong Synod of the Church of Christ in China.

    The Assistant Colonial Secretary, James S. Lockhart, noted the significant cultural and societal differences between the New Territories and urban Hong Kong. These differences presented unique challenges for missionary work, reflecting the NTES‚Äôs cross-cultural and collaborative efforts.

    (9) St. Andrew‚Äôs Church, 1906 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 159-161; St. Andrew‚Äôs Church n.d.; Vessy 2004)

    Following the cession of the Kowloon Peninsula to Britain in 1860, Tsim Sha Tsui was developed into a garden city reserved for Western residents. In 1899, Anglican services began at the Seamen‚Äôs Club. With funding from Sir Catchick Paul Chater, St. Andrew‚Äôs Church was completed in 1906 on Nathan Road. It became the first Christian church in the area, serving the expatriate community.

    The Gothic Revival church, constructed with red bricks, was consecrated in 1904 by Bishop Joseph Hoare. Its bell tower originally featured a spire, which was removed in 1966 due to corrosion. Near the stone walls of the church, inscriptions by Bishop Ronald Hall remain visible, bearing the words: ‚ÄúThe Lord Hath Need of You.‚Äù

    (10) Pentecostal Mission, 1907 (Yip Sin-Chuen 2021; Au 2019)

    In 1907, Alfred G. Garr and his wife introduced the Pentecostal movement to Hong Kong following their experience at the Azusa Street Revival in 1906. Initially, they rented a property on Ladder Street, which also served as a missionary dormitory, and began holding Pentecostal meetings in collaboration with Charles R. Hager of the Congregational Church. The meetings emphasized repentance and baptism by the Holy Spirit.

    Due to opposition, Garr relocated the meetings to a private location, and in 1907, the Apostolic Faith Mission was established. After the deaths of their two daughters in 1908, the Garrs temporarily left Hong Kong but returned in 1909. By 1910, the church was renamed the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission, emphasizing its status as a self-supporting Chinese church. It expanded to Guangzhou, Zhongshan, and other locations under the leadership of Chinese pastors, becoming a foundation for Pentecostalism in Southern China.

    (11) Fan Hing Christian Association, 1907 (Lau Yuet-Sang 1996: 278-281; Yeung Kwok-Keung 2010: 64-65)

    The Fan Hing Christian Association was founded in 1907 by three Anglican believers, Wu Tin-Bo, Yeung Siu-Chuen, and Wu Kwing-Nam. Its mission was to enhance members‚Äô spiritual growth and encourage participation in social services. Initially, its membership mainly comprised Anglican congregants. However, over time, members from different denominations joined. Starting in 1920, the association‚Äôs chairperson was no longer required to have an Anglican background and was instead elected annually by the members.

    As the association expanded, its organizational structure developed to include several departments: Moral Education, Intellectual Development, Social Interaction, Membership Affairs, Charity, Publications, and General Administration, reflecting similarities to the YMCA model. In 1914, the association began publishing Fan Hing News, edited by Yeung Siu-Chuen.

    (1) Ma Ying-Biu and the Founding of Sincere Department Store, 1900

    (Lee Chi-Kwong 2006: 13-30; Wong Kin-Fai 2015)

    Ma Ying-Biu (1864‚Äì1944), born in Xiangshan, Guangdong, in 1864, worked as a miner in Australia before founding the Wing Sang Company in Sydney. In 1894, he was baptized at the Sydney Chinese Presbyterian Church and later moved to Hong Kong, where he married Ho Hing-Tong, the second daughter of Anglican pastor Ho Ching-Shan. He also established Wing Cheong Gold Mountain Firm. In Hong Kong, Ma participated in the Anglican Church and was a founding member of the Chinese YMCA of Hong Kong. In 1900, he established Sincere Company, the first Western-style department store in Hong Kong, located on Queen‚Äôs Road. Ma emphasized ‚Äúbusiness as a means of spreading the gospel.‚Äù He passed away in Hong Kong in 1944.

    The initial shareholders of Sincere Company included 13 overseas Chinese merchants from Australia, the Americas, and Xiangshan, many of whom were members of the To Tsai Church or the Chinese Congregational Church and also served as directors of the Chinese YMCA. The company established the Moral Education Department (1907), Intellectual Education Department (1911), and Hui Ai Society (1922). The Moral Education Department aimed to cultivate employees‚Äô ethics and spread Christian teachings. The Intellectual Education Department offered evening classes to train business talent, while the Hui Ai Society collected donations from staff to fund charitable activities.

    As a devout Christian, Ma ensured that Sincere Department Store closed on Sundays to allow employees to attend church services. The store set a precedent for the modern department store industry, earning Ma the title ‚ÄúFather of Chinese Department Stores.‚Äù Sincere was followed by the establishment of other major stores in Hong Kong, including True Light Department Store, Wing On Department Store (1907, founded by Kwok Chuen of the Chinese Congregational Church), and The Sun Company (1911, founded by Choi Cheong and Choi Hing of the Hop Yat Church). These ‚ÄúFour Major Stores‚Äù were all operated by Christian businessmen returning from Australia and America.

    From December 1922 to January 1923, during John Mott‚Äôs visit to Hong Kong, the Four Stores‚Äô managers hosted a banquet at the Hongkong Hotel for Mott and local church leaders (Wah Tsz Yat Po, 1923).

    (2) John R. Mott‚Äôs Evangelistic Visit to Hong Kong, 1901

    (Yip Cham-Ming 2014: 88)

    John R. Mott (1865‚Äì1955), General Secretary of the World Student Christian Federation (W.S.C.F.), was a long-time leader of the YMCA and the Student Volunteer Movement for Foreign Missions. During his 1901 visit to China, Mott spent two days in Hong Kong. Local Chinese churches organized two large-scale youth-focused evangelistic meetings.

    The first meeting was held at Queen‚Äôs College, attracting around 400 to 500 attendees. Since the colonial government prohibited religious activities in government schools, follow-up counseling sessions were held at To Tsai Church, resulting in eight young people converting to Christianity. The second meeting drew 600 to 700 attendees, with 57 conversions. This marked the first large-scale gospel movement in Hong Kong.

    (3) Joint Publication of The Chinese Christian Monthly, 1908

    (Hing Fook-Tsang 2014b: 229-230)

    Published in 1908, The Chinese Christian Monthly was a collaborative effort by three German missionary societies: the Basel Mission, the Rhenish Mission (Barmen), and the Berlin Mission. Edited by I. Genahr, a missionary of the Hong Kong Rhenish Church, the journal was produced at the Rhenish Mission office in Hong Kong and published twice a month.

    The journal‚Äôs mission statement emphasized fostering unity among the three societies, likening their cooperation to ‚Äúa connecting cord gradually bringing harmony.‚Äù Content included theological discussions, church news, biographies, novels, and general reports. Most contributors were Chinese pastors affiliated with the three societies, such as Mak Mui-Sang, Wong Yuen-Shum, and Cheung Sing-Wah.

    The Basel and Rhenish Missions were active in Hong Kong and Guangdong Province, with strong denominational ties reflected in the journal‚Äôs content. However, from July 1908, the journal expanded its reach by involving other denominations, such as the Wesleyan Church (represented by Chan Chuk-Nam) and To Tsai Church (represented by Cheung Chuk-Ling).

    Despite these efforts, The Chinese Christian Monthly ceased publication at the end of 1911 after four years. Subscription numbers had declined from a peak of 1,200 to fewer than 1,000, and editor Genahr planned to return to Germany in the spring of 1912, leaving no successor to continue the work.

    (1) Non-Mainstream Pentecostal Movement

    (Yip Sin-Chuen 2021; Dai Koon-Ho 2020; Au 2019)

    In the 1900s, alongside the Azusa Street Revival, the Pentecostal movement gained momentum, with Pentecostal missionaries arriving in Hong Kong. Undoubtedly, Hong Kong became one of the key regions for introducing the Pentecostal movement to China (Bays 1995: 129).

    The Pentecostal movement in Hong Kong began in 1907 when Alfred G. Garr and his wife arrived, leading to the establishment of the Hong Kong Pentecostal Mission, a Chinese self-supporting church founded by Mok Lai-Chi and Sung Ting-Man. By the end of 1909, Anna M. Deane (?‚Äì1918) of the Tabernacle Pentecostal Church arrived in Hong Kong and gradually focused on ministering to fishermen, founding the American Pentecostal Mission, later renamed the Pentecostal Holiness Church.

    Between the 1920s and 1940s, other Pentecostal denominations, including the Assemblies of God, the Apostolic Faith Mission, and the Foursquare Gospel Church, established their presence in Hong Kong. These developments created a diverse Pentecostal landscape in the city.

    During the early 20th century, the Pentecostal movement faced significant resistance from mainstream churches in Hong Kong, often labeled as heretical. However, by the 1920s, it began to gain acceptance from traditional denominations. According to a brief history of Pentecostal churches (Hong Kong Chinese Christian Churches Union 1993: 189), ‚ÄúIn the early days, this church was mistakenly regarded by other believers in Hong Kong as heretical and was isolated. However, by 1927, the Hong Kong Christian Churches Union formally invited the church to join as a member congregation.‚Äù

      (1) **Conflict Between Evangelism and Traditional Culture in the

      New Territories**

      (Lu Pui-Yee 2009)

      After the establishment of the New Territories Evangelization Society, the mission inevitably encountered resistance from traditional village religious cultures during its evangelistic efforts. For example, the 1910 annual report mentioned significant challenges faced by missionaries preaching in Sheung Shui. In one village, a family that chose to convert to Christianity was ostracized by other villagers. The father and son of the family were unable to sustain their livelihood within the village.

      The 1912 report further noted that there remained strong opposition to Christianity in the villages of Sheung Shui. A colporteur who traveled around Yuen Long distributing religious books, along with his family, was driven out by villagers. The house they lived in was even barricaded.

      Later, tensions arose between the New Territories Evangelization Society and villagers in Tsuen Wan. At the time, not every household owned weighing tools for goods, so public scales were provided for villagers to weigh items such as pineapples and pork. Villagers paid a fee for this service, and the collected funds were used to maintain local temples. However, some villagers who converted to Christianity objected to their money being used to repair what they considered ‚Äúpagan‚Äù temples and refused to pay the fees. This refusal sparked dissatisfaction and led to boycotts by other villagers.

      The tensions and conflicts between the New Territories Evangelization Society and traditional village society illustrate the inevitable challenges faced by Christianity as it sought to spread within Chinese communities.
